Received 
      1st August 2022
    , Accepted 25th October 2022First published on 2nd November 2022AbstractGraphene and its derivatives (graphene oxide (GO), reduced GO (rGO), doped graphene, and functionalized graphene (F-graphene) nanomaterials (NMs)) possess unique chemical and physical features, and thus huge efforts have been devoted to their study, achieving substantial advances in fuel cell (FC) applications. Herein, we provide a summary of the latest performance and progress in the synthesis, properties, and application of graphene-based NMs in membranes, bipolar plates (BPPs), and as electrocatalysts in various types of FC technological applications. Specifically, in this review, we explain in detail the effect of introducing an increased electrochemically active specific surface area (SSA) to achieve better electro-catalytic activity toward fuel oxidation and oxidant reduction processes using graphene-based NM dispersions on electrodes. Using materials based on graphene, the problem of the device stack durability and performance degradation brought by the corrosion of the BPPs is highlighted. Graphene-based NMs have demonstrated outstanding performances in different membrane devices with respect to high ionic conductivity and power density. The use of graphene in FC technology has demonstrated admirable performances, and thus has a promising future for industrial applications.
1. Introduction
Concern for the increasing energy demand globally and environmental pollution due to the use of fossil fuels has fuelled the development of renewable and eco-friendly energy alternatives. A renewable hydrogen economy strategy, involving hydrogen generation, storage, and transfer of its chemical energy to electricity is broadly considered a potential solution for the future of renewable energy production and environmentally safe consumption through fuel cell (FC) technology.1,2 FCs are crucial in hydrogen economy for introducing low carbon-based ground and aerial transportation, where it is predicted that the well-to-wheels greenhouse gas emissions will be reduced to almost zero after hydrogen is employed to produce renewable energy. Renewable and environmentally friendly sources of energy have attracted global attention due to the need to address the increasing energy demand and environmental pollution due to the use of conventional fossil fuels. Among them, hydrogen economy based on renewable energy sources is widely considered a possible energy future option. However, this technology must be efficient and environmentally beneficial.2 FCs are a type of future novel energy conversion technology that generates electricity when a hydrogen-based fuel source is applied to the system. Given that the chemical energy of hydrogen-based fuels is directly transformed to electricity, FCs are more efficient systems than combustion engines, also reducing the pollutant emissions. Therefore, FCs are regarded as one of the most interesting future technologies for addressing the global energy and environmental concerns and making our lives cleaner and more sustainable.3–17
Generally, a conventional FC consists of an electrolyte (in liquid/solid form) layer sandwiched between two electrodes (Fig. 1(a)).18 The anode surface oxidizes the applied fuel and activated electrons (“e−s”) pass through an external circuit to reduce oxygen at the cathode. To complete the circuit, the produced mobile charge carriers (H+, OH−, CO32−, or O2−) pass through the electrolyte simultaneously. Generally, FCs are categorized into five types based on the electrolytes used in the devices, i.e., phosphoric acid FC (PAFC), polymer electrolyte membrane FC (PEMFC), alkaline FC (AFC), molten carbonate FC (MCFC), and solid-oxide FC (SOFC). PAFC, PEMFC, and AFC typically function at a relatively low temperature (<300 °C) and are appropriate for use in cars and portable applications, whereas MCFC and SOFC may use a variety of fuels owing to their high working temperature (≥500 °C), making them more suitable for stationary applications.18
 Fig. 1  (a) Scheme of FCs incorporating graphene-based NMs in each component18 and (b and c) estimated areas for the use of FCVs and BEVs in near-future automotive transportation and evaluation of their technological characteristics, respectively. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 19 Copyright (2021), Springer Nature. 
In the next stage, the conversion of hydrogen into electricity is employed to run FC vehicles (FCVs), which are essential for providing low-carbon transportation in the hydrogen economy, and well-to-wheels greenhouse gas emissions should be lowered to almost zero when hydrogen is produced from renewable sources.20,21 FCVs and battery electric vehicles (BEVs) are frequently compared as the two low-carbon transportation options.22 FCs are energy conversion devices that generally utilize hydrogen as a form of stored energy, whereas batteries are energy storage devices. Due to the higher energy density and faster refuelling times of FCVs than lithium ion batteries (LIBs), hydrogen offers intrinsic benefits as a storage medium. Because BEVs frequently display a significantly lower discharge capacity,23 FCVs are also superior to BEVs in sub-zero temperature settings. In the case of short-range cars (less than 200 miles or 322 km), the FCV costs are currently higher than that of BEVs, whereas for long-range vehicles, especially those with large yearly production rates, the FCV costs are equivalent to or cheaper than that of BEVs (above 300 miles or 483 km).24,25 However, the delivery efficiency of FCVs is significantly lower compared to BEVs, and hydrogen storage and the required infrastructure are still in their infancy.26,27
The general consensus is that FCVs are suitable for heavy-duty and long-distance transportation, and for other commercial vehicles such as forklifts, whereas BEVs are appropriate for light-duty and short-distance transportation (Fig. 1(b) and (c)).28 This is based on the differences in their technical characteristics. The fast technological advancement of LIBs over the past 20-years, together with grid at-home recharging has enabled the BEV industry to expand significantly. Alternatively, in the case of FCVs, there are technological challenges associated with PEMFCs, which are their main part. Additionally, the larger-scale commercialization of FCVs is significantly impacted by the performance, price, and durability of the PEMFC stack. Thus, the development of FCVs is essential to increase their power density.22,29 The second-generation MIRAI (Toyota FCV, 2020) achieved stack power densities of 4.4 kW l−1 and 5.4 kW l−1, an increase of 42% and 54% compared to that realized in the earlier generation, respectively.30 The stack power density objectives for automotive use are about 6.0 kW l−1 and 9.0 kW l−1, respectively, in accordance with Japan's New Energy and Industrial Technology Development Organization (Japan NEDO). A PEMFC stack with a power density of 5.38 kW l−1 (with end plates), current density of 2.67 A cm−2, and single-cell voltage of 0.6 V was recently shown by the European Union FC and Hydrogen 2 Joint Undertaking (EU-FCH2JU), whose target is 9.3 kW l−1 by 2024.19,31
By producing electricity through the electrochemical oxidation of renewable alcoholic fuels such as glucose, ethanol (EtOH), methanol (CH4O), and gaseous fuel hydrogen created by water splitting, FCs can minimize the demand for fossil fuels. Besides the traditional FC electro-catalysts, for instance mono- and bimetallic expensive metal electrocatalysts, which exhibit excellent catalytic activity, graphene, MXene, and graphdiyne have become very attractive options as electrocatalysts due to their easy modification.1,2,32 Regardless of the use of 2D or 3D graphene structure, 2D graphene-based electrocatalysts can be divided into two categories. The first is anodic electrocatalysts for the oxidation of hydrogen in hydrogen-based FCs (HFCs) or anodic electrocatalysts for alcoholic fuels (CH4O, EtOH, and glucose) in alcoholic-FCs. The second is related to cathodic electrocatalysts for the oxygen reduction reaction (ORR) in alcoholic FCs and HFCs. Recently, significant research efforts have been dedicated to this field, mainly focusing on use of novel electrocatalytic materials in FCs. However, the material selection for the components of FC devices is complicated by issues including low electrochemical performance, efficiency, and durability. Consequently, the superior chemical, electrical, and mechanical properties of graphene and its derivatives make them suitable alternative materials for application in FC devices. In the following section we discuss the structure, synthesis techniques and applications of graphene-based NMs in detail.
2. Structure and properties of graphene
Generally, graphene is a member of the carbon family, where the application of carbon materials (CMs) spans several different disciplines. Graphene-based nanomaterials (NMs) continue to attract attention and are regarded as promising materials for application in electronics, including FCs and other renewable energy devices, due to their abundant nature. This is because of the distinct electrical and chemical properties of these CMs. CM-based electrode materials are attractive compared to other materials because of their abundant resources, flexible nature of their bonds, light weight, cost-effectiveness, and environmental friendliness. Carbon-based molecules play a significant role in supporting life on Earth. However, a “low-carbon” lifestyle is important. Carbon is a very attractive element due to its three different hybridizations, i.e., sp, sp2, and sp3 (Fig. 2).33 Thus, in the field of synthetic organic chemistry, research on full CMs has a surged in the last 20 years.
 Fig. 2  Graphene-based derivatives. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 34. Copyright (1969), Elsevier. 
Recently, the design and synthesis of potential low-dimension CMs have become the focus of scientists/researchers because of their abundant nature. Currently, new 2D CMs are being produced by researchers, where the applications of these materials depend on the types of carbon atoms that they possess with a range of morphologies, such as, fullerenes (0D, 1985),35 carbon nanotubes (1D, CNTs, 1991),36 and graphene (2D, 2004).37 Over the past two decades, a variety of carbon allotropes, including CNTs, fullerene (sp2), G (sp2), and diamond (sp3), have been produced via the hybridization of different carbon atoms. Several applications using these materials have been executed successfully. Herein, we only focus only on 2D graphene-based NMs.
Graphene, a 2D NS composed of sp2 linked single-layer (SL) carbon-atoms with a honeycomb lattice structure, has sparked significant interest in the advanced nanotechnological world due to its broad novel application potential. For a long time, it was believed that the graphene-NMs, as single-atomic thick 2D nanosheets (NSs), served as the building block of all other dimensional carbon-compounds and 2DMs.38,39 A few of the notable novel chemical and physical properties of G NMs include high mechanical strength (1 TPa),40 high conductivity,41 and large specific surface area (SSA) (2675 m2 g−1),42 which may rival or even outperform other materials especially single-walled carbon-nanotubes (SW-CNTs) and multi-walled carbon-nanotubes (MW-CNTs).
Graphene doped with foreign components has become crucial in the study of green energy production. Thus, in recent years, significant attempts have been made to capitalize on potential use of graphene-based NMs in FCs. Research on the electrocatalytic applications of 2D graphene-based NMs in FCs is a prominent area in the development of green energy.8–17,43–74 Specifically, graphene-based NMs are good electrocatalyst supports given that they boost the number of active sites and facilitate electron transport in both fuel oxidation and ORR. The primary reason for this is that the modification of graphene NMs often improve the interaction between the graphene nanostructure and loaded materials. The electrocatalytic and electrochemical characteristics of graphene NMs have been thoroughly investigated. The most recent evaluations mostly concentrate on a single application sector related to FCs, such as the ORR, fuel oxidation, membranes, and bipolar plates (BPPs). Because of their good catalytic activity, great tolerance to poisoning, and low cost, metal-free graphene NMs have been shown to be viable candidates for the ORR. Extensive insights have been achieved into the impact of changes in the electrical structure of graphene, doping structures, defects, and functional groups on the performance of FCs. Furthermore, the integration of graphene NMs in polymer membranes increases their ionic conductivity and reduces the fuel crossover. Graphene NMs have strong proton conductivity and are impermeable to water, hydrogen, and methanol as proton-exchange membranes (PEMs). Furthermore, graphene NMs can enhance the current collection, fuel/air distribution, and stability of BPPs in addition to the electrolyte and electrode.
Although several early systematic studies explored graphene NMs and their applications in each FC component, a summary of the most recent discoveries in this field is lacking. Thus, in this review paper, we describe the important functions of graphene-based NMs in the FC electrodes, membranes, electrolytes, and BPPs, highlighting the recent breakthroughs such as 3D graphene-based electrodes and graphene-supported single-atom electrocatalysts. Furthermore, the essential elements influencing the performance of these graphene-based NMs in FCs are carefully investigated. Subsequently, the fundamental mechanics and design techniques are thoroughly examined. Finally, their limits and future research potential are discussed. Here, initially, we summarize the factors affecting the structure and properties of graphene in addition to the methods for their synthesis.
2.1. Structure of graphene nanomaterials
The structure of graphene is basically a layer of graphite that is roughly 0.34 nm thick or one atom thick. The size of an SL of graphite, which may range from nanometers (nm) to centimeters (cm), is determined by the method applied for its synthesis.75 A single graphitic layer may be transformed into 0D fullerene, 1D CNT, or 3D graphite by stacking on top of another layer.38 Mono-atomic or SL graphene refers to an SL of graphite, whereas bilayer (BL) and tri-layer refer to two and three layers of graphene, respectively. For example, few-layer (FL) 2D graphite (FL-graphene), which has 5 to 10 layers, is distinguished from multi-layer (ML), thick graphite, with 20 to 30 layers using terms such nanocrystalline thin graphite.76 It should be noted that larger layered formations should not be considered 2D graphene crystals, but rather as thin film 3D-type graphite.77 This type of graphite, known as SL graphite, is composed of covalently functionalized, sp2 linked carbon atoms organized in a honeycomb-like hexagonal lattice, where van der Waals (vdWs) forces bind the stack layers of graphene in graphite. SL graphene naturally occurs in a folded state similar to a corrugated NS with no stacking due to changes in the temperature.39 In contrast, FL graphene NMs demonstrate a preference for the coexistence of “AB” stacking and “ABC” (with rhombohedral arrangement) stacking, while double-layer 2D graphene NSs exhibit Bernal (“AB”) stacking rather than “AAA” stacking.39,78 Also, different properties will result from different structures, stacking structures, and layer counts.75 Thus, to control the structure of graphene, it is essential to understand its electronic dislocations.79 The mobility of the carbon atoms inside the unit cell is crucial for the continuous growth of nanostructured graphene. Significant efforts have been devoted to obtaining a nanostructured array with particular size, shape, crystallinity, and functionality. Consequently, these materials have been extensively used in electronics, optical devices, electrochemical energy conversion/storage, solar energy harvesting, and other applications. Consequently, several inorganic nanostructures have been combined with 2D graphene NMs to improve their characteristics, which will be explained in detail in the following sections.
2.2. Properties of graphene-based nanomaterials
High strength (130 GPa), high thermal conductivity (3000 W m−1 K−1), rapid theoretical SSA (2600 m2 g−1), and high charge mobility (230000 cm2 V−1 s−1) are a few of the intriguing characteristics of 2D graphene NMs.78 Thus, the scientific community has been inspired to learn more about the uses of 2D graphene NMs as a result of these characteristics. Therefore, a thorough understanding of the application of 2D graphene NMs based on their chemical, physical, mechanical, and electrochemical characteristics is necessary for FC applications.

2.2.1. Electrical property of graphene. 
The electrical characteristics of 2D graphene NMs are well understood. Graphene has Dirac points at the K and K′ sites in reciprocal lattice space, where the unoccupied conduction band (CB) (*π-band) and occupied valence band (VB) (π-band) come into contact, with slight overlap between the VB and CB (Fig. 3(a)).80,81 Consequently, pristine graphene is a unique type of intrinsic semiconductor that acts like Dirac fermions, which are massless relativistic particles (Fig. 3(b)).82 Different numbers of graphene layers result in various band structures, appearance of charge carriers, and overlap between the CB and VB. Due to its parabolic spectrum and incredibly tiny band overlap (1.6 meV), BL graphene can be described as a zero band-gap (B.G) semiconductor with one type of electrons and hole. However, given that charge carriers arise in three or more graphene layers, the band structure becomes considerably more complicated, and the CB and VB begin to noticeably overlap (Fig. 3(c)).83 The electronic conductivity of graphene completely determines its quality; consequently, the higher the conductivity, the better the quality (i.e., lower defect density in its crystal lattice). Defects are used as scattering sites to restrict the mean free path of electrons, preventing charge flow. The immaculate graphene created from 3D materials is fundamentally stable, indicating that no issues will arise even at high temperature, according to Geim and Novoselov.38 Surface charge traps, substrate ripples, and interfacial phonons are examples of the extrinsic sources that have an impact on the conductivity of graphene NMs.84–86
 Fig. 3  (a) CB of 2D graphene touches the VB at the K and K′ points. (b) Linear band dispersion relation near the Dirac points with zero B.G nature.87 (c) Electronic and optical properties of various layers of graphene. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 88. Copyright (2010), the American Chemical Society. 
The resulting charge carrier mobility from various graphene extraction procedures is presented in Table 1.93 The ambipolar electric field effect of 2D graphene NMs, which allows the charge carriers to be constantly tuned as electrons and holes even under ambient conditions, is another essential feature.80 In field-effect devices, a positive gate bias raises the Fermi level over the Dirac point and promotes holes to the VB at concentrations of n = αVg (where “α” is a coefficient dependent on the SiO2 layer employed as a dielectric and Vg is the gate voltage).81 The charge mobility of graphene on an insulating substrate is ∼1.5 × 104 cm2 V−1 s−1 at 300 K and 6 × 104 cm2 V−1 s−1 at 4 K, with charge concentrations of up to 1013 cm2 (combined electrons and holes). Compared to silicon (Si) (1.5 × 1013 cm2 V−1 s−1), suspended graphene has a low-temperature mobility that can approach 2 × 105 cm2 V−1 s−1 and even exceed 2.5 × 105 cm2 V−1 s−1.94,95 The charge carriers in graphene NMs can be detected through the special quantum Hall effect (QHE) and Berry's phase.96 The sheet resistance (Rs) is minimal (30 V sq−1), which is a result of the remarkable conductivity of graphene.81

2.2.2. Thermal properties of graphene. 
Graphene NMs are well known as being good conductors of heat and electricity, depending on their category and modified structure, attracting much interest in theoretical and experimental science and industry. Graphene NMs are frequently used in electronic equipment due to the increased performance and reliability of the resulting electrical components; however, their thermal management should be considered. The maximum thermal conductivity of SL graphene and supported graphene at room temperature (RT) was reported to be 5000 W m−1 K−1 and 600 W m−1 K−1, respectively.81 There are numerous reports in the literature on the conductivity of graphene NMs in last few years. For instance, Castro Neto et al.97 studied the essential theoretical issues related to graphene NMs using peculiar 2D Dirac-like electronic excitation. Ma et al.98 reported the current developments of graphene nanoribbons (G-NRs), particularly the longitudinal unzipping of CNTs. In their study, they also included a brief summary of the mechanical, electrical, magnetic, and edge reconstruction properties of capabilities graphene-NRs. The potential development of research on novel graphene NMs including their synthesis, characteristics, applications, and limitations of the current body of knowledge and future research paths was the focus of the study be Choi et al.99 To ensure uniform outcomes, the estimated heat conductivity of graphene NM was recorded. For instance, Baladin et al.100 and Ghosh101 measured the thermal conductivity of graphene-NM using Raman spectroscopy, which was determined to be 4840–5300 W m−1 K−1 (2–5 m diameter) and 3080–5150 W m−1 K−1 (1–5 m diameter), respectively. Additionally, according to Lee et al.,102 the thermal conductivity of suspended pristine graphene NMs ranged from 1800 W m−1 K−1 (at 325 K) to 710 W m−1 K−1 when employing the same experimental conditions (at 500 K). The thermal conductivity of a suspended graphene disk with a diameter of 44 mm was tested using the same approach by Faugeras et al.,103 which was determined to be 632 W m−1 K−1. The conductivity achieved by Balandin was around 8-times that reported by Faugeras et al.100 despite the fact that Faugeras utilized a suspended disk with a diameter that was 9-times larger than that of the 2D graphene-based NMs used by Balandin et al.100 Other testing techniques, including scanning thermal microscopy and use of micro electro-thermal system, in addition to Raman spectroscopy, revealed that the thermal conductivity of graphene NMs is in the range of 10–25 W m−1 K−1. Theoretical estimations indicate that they have a thermal conductivity in the range of 25 to 20000 W m−1 K−1.104Table 2 displays the thermal conductivity of graphene NMs measured under various conditions using Raman spectroscopy, micro electrothermal method, and scanning thermal microscopy. Clearly, the thermal conductivity of graphene shows inconsistent results. This is because several factors, including sample size, synthesis technique, structure, and temperature have an impact on the thermal conductivity of graphene NMs.

2.2.3. Optical properties of graphene. 
The optical characteristics of pristine graphene NMs are distinct because of their linear band structure, zero B.G, and intense interaction of Dirac fermions with electromagnetic radiation. Graphene-based NMs absorb in a broad spectrum range, which is due to their inter-band and intra-band optical transitions. The absorption of graphene NMs from the visible (vis.,) to near-infrared (NIR) range is characterized by inter-band transitions and is frequency independent, as defined by the fine structure constant. Intra-band transitions or free carrier absorption are responsible for the optical response in the far-IR range. Because of the momentum mismatch, direct photon absorption cannot be achieved via intra-band transition. Phonon scattering occurs to conserve momentum, and subsequently a population inversion of free carriers appears around the K-point. The free carrier absorption conductivity has a Drude-like frequency dependence. Plasmonic excitation of charge carriers can modify the far-IR sensitivity to the terahertz region. However, due to the huge momentum mismatch in photons and plasmons, plasmon-assisted light absorption is not permitted in graphene. Alternatively, electrostatic gate coupling and creating graphene nanostructures can stimulate plasmon excitation.140
A SL of graphene absorbs about 2.3% of incident light in a wide frequency range, from far-IR to vis., according to several studies on the optical response of graphene.141,142 The fine structure constant, a = e2/hc ≈ 1/37 (c = speed of light), provides a straightforward formula for the opacity of suspended graphene.143 Additionally, it has been discovered that the opacity increases with the membrane thickness; consequently, each graphene layer contributes an additional 2.3%, as illustrated in Fig. 4(a). The “G-opacity” is nearly wavelength independent (Fig. 4(b)).143 Although the absorbance of SL graphene is flat between 300 and 2500 nm, it exhibits a peak at 250 nm in the UV region because of the inter-band electronic transition from its unoccupied π*-states (Fig. 4(c)).144 The optical transition of graphene can also be altered by altering the Fermi energy.145 Electrical gating and charge injection in graphene optoelectronic devices were projected to lead to the development of tuneable IR detectors, modulators, and emitters.146 There has been significant interest in new photonic devices by combing the optical and electrical transport capabilities of graphene. Additionally, SL and FL graphene NMs with zero B.G and huge area can be used as ultrafast photodetectors, according to a previous discussion.147
 Fig. 4  (a) Evaluation of the light absorption of BL and SL graphene. The layer thickness is 20 μm. (b) Opacity of graphene is nearly independent of the wavelength.141 (c) Transmittance of different graphene layers.151 (d) Structure of GO representing sp2 and sp3 domains. (e) IR spectra of GO, rGO-180 and rGO-400. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 140. Copyright (2021), Springer Nature. 
The density of electrons and holes, temperature, and light absorption on the surface can affect the generation of electron and hole pairs, which can recombine very quickly (picoseconds).148 Additionally, when external and internal fields are applied close to the electrode and graphene contact, the electrons and holes may be separated, producing a photocurrent.147,149 Another feature of graphene NMs has been suggested to be photoluminescence. It is feasible to make graphene NMs luminous by introducing an appropriate B.G. Specifically, two methods have been proposed to achieve this, where the first technique involves breaking graphene NSs into NRs and quantum dots (QDs), while the second technique involves physically or chemically treating the electron network of graphene to reduce its association with other gases.150 For instance, photoluminescence can be generated by treating SL graphene on a substrate with oxygen plasma. Consequently, etching the top layer without damaging the underlying layer can create a hybrid structure.150 The structure of GO-based NMs is exceedingly disordered and shows various oxygen functional groups, namely, hydroxyls and epoxy, on the basal plane and a tiny quantity of carboxyls, carbonyls, and lactones at the margins, resulting in low electrical conductivity. The structure of GO NMs is shown in Fig. 4(d). The presence of these oxygen-rich functional groups has certain advantages for employing GO in the production of optoelectronic devices. Because of the presence of oxygen functional groups in GO, it is hydrophilic, forming stable dispersions in polar solvents and serving as a precursor for the production of graphene. GO-based NM dispersions may be placed on any hydrophilic surface. Fig. 4(e) depicts the IR spectra of GO, where the very significant band in the range of 3000–3700 cm−1 is caused by the stretching modes of the adsorbed/intercalated water and hydroxyls and carboxyls. The other GO vibrational modes are related to oxygen-containing functional groups, such as ketonic species (1650–1750 cm−1) and carboxyls (1600–1650 cm−1) and asymmetric vibrational stretching of CC coupled with water bending modes (1500–1600 cm−1) and epoxides (1230–1320 cm−1 and 850 cm−1). The spectral overlap region (850–1500 cm−1) is divided into three regions, i.e., α-region (900–1100 cm−1) for all ether derivatives, β-region (1100–1280 cm−1) for ketonic species including peroxides and pyran, and γ-region (1280–1500 cm−1) for epoxides. GO NSs are made up of tetrahedral sp3 carbon atoms with oxygen groups interleaved with un-oxidized graphitic sp2 carbon atoms (Fig. 4(d)).140
The chaotic structure of GO may be partially transformed to graphene-like NSs by eliminating its functional groups and restoring a conjugated structure. Generally, GO-based thin films are insulating, with a large energy B.G in the density of states (DOSs) and sheet conductivity of 1012 Ω sq−1 or higher. Thermal annealing in a vacuum or inert gas environment, exposure to hydrazine vapor or sodium boro-hydride, or laser heating can be used to reduce solution-processed GO films. The structure of rGO is defined as nanometer-sized graphitic islands separated by oxidized graphene. This asymmetric distribution of graphitic domains and oxidized sp3 domains in rGO violates the symmetry and opens a B.G. Because there are overlapping areas of mid-gap states, the B.G is a transport gap. The optical response of rGO is determined by the size of its graphitic domains and oxygen functional group coverage. Shen et al.152 examined the optical absorption of rGO in the IR to UV range. The optical absorption in the IR and vis regions was roughly 2.5–3.5%, which is comparable to that in the graphene layer. There was a noticeable peak in the UV area, which shifted from 5.1 to 4.5 eV with a decrease in the content of rGO. Theoretical studies indicate a linear drop in the B.G as the oxygen concentration of GO decreases. The UV absorption in rGO is caused by π to π* transitions, with values of 4.8 eV and 5.2 eV when the oxygen functional group coverage was 25% and 75%, respectively. Carbonyl groups are primarily responsible for the plasmon peak shift. Un-rGO has a high concentration of hydroxyl and epoxy groups with a low concentration of carbonyl groups, whereas rGO has a higher concentration of carbonyl groups. An increase in the content of carbonyl groups creates vacancies in graphene, where an increase in vacancies causes graphene sheet tearing and a reduction in B.G compared to functionalized graphene.140
The IR spectroscopic experiments by Acik et al.153 experimentally showed the production of carbonyl groups in rGO. The IR spectrum of GO and rGO NMs annealed at 180 °C (denoted as rGO-180) was evaluated, as shown in Fig. 4(b). Thermal annealing reduced the strength of the vibrational modes in the range of 3000–3700 cm−1 by removing the hydroxyls and adsorbed/intercalated water molecules from GO. In the case of rGO-180, the water modes disappeared but the oxygen-containing species remained. The vibrational peak in proportion to the ketonic area (1650–1750 cm−1) was elevated to some extent, indicating that carbonyls are formed during the elimination of hydroxyls through a free radical reaction. After mild thermal annealing up to 200 °C, the trapped water between the neighboring layers of GO drastically impacted the chemistry of ML graphene.154 The interlayer nano-spacing of stacked GO was confined water molecules, where the production of faults and carbonyl groups in ML graphene is aided by this restricted water. Subsequently, further annealing at 400 °C eliminated the oxygen functional groups and trapped water, resulting in the formation of defect graphene structures (Fig. 4(e)). Consequently, controlling the reduction and trapped water enables the electrical and optical characteristics of GO to be adjusted.140


2.2.4. Mechanical properties of graphene. 
The outstanding mechanical characteristics of graphene NMs are a result of the stability of their sp2 bonds, which create a hexagonal lattice and avoid any deformation in the in-plane. Hone and colleagues155,156 performed AFM nano-indentation (Fig. 5(a)) to assess the mechanical characteristics of free-standing SL graphene for the first time. The authors employed a force displacement response from graphene NMs with the intention of determining their elastic characteristics and breaking stress (Fig. 5(b)). Consequently, it was discovered that the force-displacement curves were independent of the tip radius, whereas the breaking force was susceptible to the tip radius but not membrane size. The maximum stress can be calculated using the equation σ2Dm = (FE2D/4πR)1/2 (where E2D is second-order elastic stiffness, R is the tip radius, and F is the applied force). The use of this equation results in the construction of third-order elasticity, which is related to the breaking force, leading to the calculated breaking strength of 55 N m−1, which cannot be regarded as correct. Assuming that the effective thickness is about 0.335 nm, this equation demonstrates that graphene NMs have a Young modulus of E = 1.0–0.1 TPa. Additionally, Hone and colleagues tested the intrinsic strength of defect-free SL graphene NMs, and the results suggested that the breaking strength of the SL membrane was 42 N m−1, which is equal to a strength of 130 GPa.156 For the investigation of the mechanical characteristics of graphene NMs and their application, a variety of methods have been used but more experiments should be conducted in the future.
 Fig. 5  (a) Nano-indentation system in suspended SL graphene membrane. (b) Loading/unloading curve with rising depth. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 156 and 157. Copyright (1989), Elsevier. 

3. Techniques used to synthesize graphene-based nanomaterials
The number of layers, defect and impurity content, solubility, lateral size, and surface chemistry are a few examples of the attributes, morphology, and structure of graphene NMs that are dependent on the utilized synthetic process. Fig. 6 demonstrates the two types of methods for the synthesis of graphene NMs, where one is the top-down and the other is the bottom-up-synthesis technique with almost 7 sub-classes of graphene synthesis techniques, which are further divided into 6 other classes. Based on these synthesis classes the obtained results are listed in Table 3, which also summarizes the advantages/disadvantages of several techniques for the synthesis of graphene NMs. The methods that are most commonly employed including mechanical exfoliation, chemical synthesis, epitaxial growth of graphene, wet chemical synthesis, and chemical vapour deposition (CVD) will be covered in this study in the following coming section.

3.1. Mechanical exfoliation method

3.1.1. Micromechanical cleavage or solid-phase exfoliation. 
One of the top-down procedures is mechanical exfoliation, where a transverse or longitudinal structure is generated over a layered structure. Weak vdWs forces hold the layers of graphene together in the form of the bulk structure of graphite, where the interlayer bond distance and bond energy are 3.34 nm and 2 eV nm−2, respectively. A layer of graphite must be extracted with an external force of 300 nN m−2 or more.176 The raw graphite precursor can be exfoliated to create NFs or NSs of graphene. In 2004, the publication by Prof. Novoselov et al.177 indicated that graphene can be produced via the repetitive usage of a technique known as micromechanical cleavage, or, more frequently, the Scotch tape approach. The micromechanical approach is simple to perform and does not require any additional tools, where just a piece of adhesive is laid on surface of graphite and peeled off. The graphite layers that adhere to tape are split at the crystal plane. By pushing a clean piece of tape against the graphite layer that is adhered to another piece of tape, more micromechanical cleavage is necessary to create FL and SL graphene NMs. To create thinner layers, these two pieces of tape are separated and the process repeated to get the appropriate graphite sheet thickness.
Optical and Raman spectroscopy and AFM are frequently used to characterize graphene produced via mechanical exfoliation (Fig. 7(a)–(c)). The next step in processing graphene NSs involves pressing the graphite layers onto a substrate, for example Si or silicon oxide (Fig. 7(d) and (e)). This method may produce a SL graphene NS with a thickness of around 0.4 nm and a lateral dimension of about microns.179 However, although this approach is straightforward, it is limited with respect to the charge mobility, purity, defects, and optoelectronic sample quality. Furthermore, this approach results in a low yield and throughput. Sonication- and shearing-based techniques are also frequently used to generate the required energy needed to overcome the interactions in the graphite layers.180 The insolubility of graphite invariably results in a broad thickness dispersion and poor exfoliation effectiveness. To achieve a greater exfoliation yield, graphite can be expanded with an increased interlayer distance using a non-oxidation intercalation process.181,182 For a variety of customer uses that call for high thermal/electrical conductivity, for example, conducting additives and filler-based nanocomposites (NCs) in renewable energy applications, graphene NFs produced by liquid-phase exfoliation have shown tremendous potential, which will be discussed in the next section.
 Fig. 7  (a) Optical microscopy image of ML graphene obtained from solid-phase exfoliation. AFM results of (b) NF edge and (c) FL graphene NMs. (d) SEM results of FL graphene and (e) device in image.80 (f) Timeline of the milestones for liquid exfoliation techniques, reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 178 Copyright (2022), Springer Nature. 


3.1.2. Liquid-phase exfoliation technique. 
The importance of interfacial tension for submerging solid surfaces in liquid media has recently been highlighted. The poor solubility of a solid in a liquid is caused by the high tension at the solid and liquid interface.183 Accordingly, the layer-layer contact can be weakened by increasing the interlayer distance, where the physical agitation of dispersions should also be considered for liquid exfoliation processes.184,185 It was established in 1958 that the oxidation of graphite, also known as “graphite oxide,” may increase the interlayer distance from 3.4 to 7.0 Å. This opening of the interlayer spacing makes it feasible to scatter the individual graphite oxide layers by sonication.
The liquid exfoliation technique is an effective method for transforming pure 2D bulk materials into individual NSs in large quantities. Chemical vapor transfer (CVT) has been used to produce bulk materials since the late 1960s (Fig. 7(f)), although the majority of 2D bulk materials are now only accessible in limited numbers. The distinctive 2D character of bulk materials frequently requires their nano-dispersion into SLs, although easy exfoliation is hampered by the substantial vdWs energy of micron-scale materials. Despite the small vdWs pressure between neighboring layers, it can be difficult to exfoliate graphite into separate layers. Therefore, the vdWs forces between neighboring layers must be overcome for successful exfoliation. One of the most intriguing methods weakening the vdWs forces is liquid plunging, in which the dispersive London interactions add potential energy to the neighboring layers by decreasing the solvent medium in relation to vacuum.186
To separate the graphitic layers and graphene, it is crucial to choose solvents with a high surface tension given that they lower the interfacial tension between graphene and the solvent. Upon chemical treatment with reducing agents and thermal annealing treatment, the graphite oxide layers can be reduced to graphene NSs.187 To separate graphite into graphene-NMs without the use of surfactants, several organic solvents have been investigated, as shown in Table 4. However, employing surfactants can facilitate the exfoliation of graphene from graphite, mainly because the surfactant has elevated adsorption energy on graphene and greater interaction energy with graphene than the solvent molecules. Surfactants also help to stabilize the exfoliated graphene in organic solvents.

Generally, the structure of graphene NSs is seriously damaged during oxidation and reduction processes. However, the interlayer distance may be increased by intercalating ions and molecules in the layers to prevent this from happening. By applying a negative and positive bias, electrochemistry permits efficient cation and anion intercalation in an electrolyte solution. In liquid or vapor phase, organic solvents, alkali metals, and surfactants with surface energies comparable to that of 2DMs may also be directly intercalated. After intercalation, materials can be exfoliated into distinct 2D layers by using agitation techniques such sonication, homogenization, and microwave action. 2D NSs can be mass-produced at RT and atmospheric P using liquid exfoliation. However, this method also results in uneven NS thickness and damage that is apparently unavoidable. The surfactants utilized in this method are presented in Table 5.

3.2. Chemical synthesis methods

3.2.1. Reduction of graphene oxide to graphene nanomaterials. 
Another way to produce the required graphene is the reduction of GO NMs via the top-down approach. Initially, GO must be prepared using layered bulk graphite materials before graphene can be synthesized. The preliminary step in the fabrication of GO is the preparation of graphite oxide using oxidizing agents such as sulfuric and nitric acid to produce hydroxyl or carboxyl groups covalently bound to a planar carbon network of graphite. Several methods are used to produce GO, including the Brodie, Staudenmaier, and Staudenmaier–Hoffman–Hamdi methods. However, the Hummers' approach, in which graphite is combined with NaNO3, H2SO4, and KMnO4,188 is the preferred method for production of GO. Following the preparation of GO, the synthesis graphene NMs is the next stage, which may be accomplished using a variety of techniques, such as thermal reduction,189 solvothermal reduction,190 hydrothermal reduction,191,192 and chemical reduction.193 After exfoliation, it becomes FL or even SL GO with a high density of defects.182 GO is a key product category in the graphene industry, given that it is a water-soluble material. By further reducing GO, which can remove the majority of its oxygen-containing functional groups and partially regain its sp2-bonded carbon network,194 small-size graphene NSs can be produced. However, rGO is significantly more disordered with poorer quality than non-oxidized graphene NFs because it has many vacancy defects and Stone-Wales defects. The commercial applications of the resulting rGO include thermal dissipation coatings, conductive additives, and mechanical reinforcement for NC materials.
3.2.1.1. Thermal reduction technique. 
The removal of oxide functional groups from the surface of GO is accomplished using a technique called thermal reduction. A GO stack was reduced by Prof. Aksay's group after it was exfoliated and heated to 1050 °C, which caused its oxide functional groups to emit CO2. The authors reported that exfoliation must occur when the rate of the decomposition of the GO epoxide and hydroxyl sites exceeds the rate of diffusion of the generated gases; specifically, the yielding pressure must be sufficient to suppress the vdWs attractions keeping graphene together. The Hamaker constant was quantitatively evaluated to determine the estimated pressure needed to separate GO-based NSs. It was found that the pressure created was 1–2 orders of magnitude more than the vdWs forces keeping the GO-based NSs together during exfoliation. However, although 80% SL rGO was obtained, it had 30% mass loss, vacancies, and structural flaws, which affected its electrical and mechanical characteristics.22
3.2.1.2. Chemical reduction technique. 
The commonly accepted Lerf–Klinowski model of graphite oxide states that there are numerous oxygen-containing groups, including OH, epoxide, carboxyl and carbonyl groups, with the basal-plane of material being mostly composed of hydroxyl and epoxides. When GO is transformed into graphene NSs, its color changes from brown (GO) to black (graphene). The elimination of the oxygen-containing groups is caused by graphene-NM becoming more hydrophobic/aggregated. An increase in the C/O ratio and conductivity of graphene NMs indicates the reduction efficiency. The reducing agents employed for the chemical reduction of GO are shown in Table 6. The “well-supported” category includes substances that are often used in synthetic chemistry and react with particular oxygen groups. Alternatively, the proposed agents are novel and interact with all the oxygen moieties. The most used reducing agent for the production of graphene NMs from GO195 is hydrazine. Unfortunately, most of the reducing chemicals used, even hydrazine is hazardous. Thus, alternatives to hydrazine have been investigated, and it has been shown that ascorbic acid, better known as vitamin-C, is the only that is comparable to hydrazine with respect to effectiveness and capacity to form rGO.196 The production of rGO with vitamin-C can be performed in organic solvents such as NMP and DMF in addition to only water. Thus, vitamin-C has been revealed to be a better, safer, and more sustainable alternative to hydrazine for large-scale use.

3.3. Epitaxial synthesis of graphene-based nanomaterials
High-quality thin films with the same crystallographic orientation, fine SL controllability, and prevention of wrinkle creation on the wafer scale can also be prepared through epitaxial synthesis methods based on the development of graphene NMs on the Si face of SiC.259,260 The major uses of SiC-derived graphene NMs are still target electronics and RF transistors due to their high fabrication cost. The reuse of SiC wafers is necessary due to their high price, with the incorporation of additional devices. The SiC substrate undergoes thermal breakdown, which results in epitaxial growth of graphene-based NMs.261 The fabrication of graphene NMs was achieved on the 0001 (Si-terminated) and 0001 (carbon-terminated) sides of 4H-SiC and 6H-SiC, respectively (Fig. 8).
 Fig. 8  (a) CVD technique for the formation of graphene NMs, (b) four basic steps of the development of graphene NMs on (c) Ni262 and (d) Cu. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 263. Copyright (2013), the American Chemical Society. 
When SiC substrates are subjected to heat treatment at about 1300 °C, Si will sublimate,264 which is frequently carried out under ultra-high vacuum (UHV) to avoid contamination.261 Given that the carbon-atoms are prevented from achieving a better rearrangement on the SiC (0001) face, a high sublimation rate of Si prevents the creation of good-feature graphene NMs, which leads to the nucleation and roughening of tiny graphene NFs.261 Emtsev et al.265 conducted an experiment using multiple heating temperature ranges in an environment with a high Ar-pressure. They noticed that the surface morphology improved when Ar was present. This is because SiC prevents the removal of the Si atoms, which lowers the rate of sublimation.265 The gradual development of the SiC (0001) face ends when extremely thin samples, up to a SL, are created during a brief period at a high temperature. However, the development of the SiC 0001 face does not self-terminate and lacks the ability to precisely regulate the sample thickness, resulting in thick samples (about 5–100 layers).266 The other methods to achieve the epitaxial growth of graphene on SiC substrates include segregating carbon-atoms from metal substrates (such as Ni, Pt, and Ru) and decomposing hydrocarbon gas on other carbide substrates (such as TaC, TiC, and WC).81
3.4. Wet chemical synthesis technique
The representative wet chemical production techniques include hydrothermal and solvothermal synthesis, where substances are dissolved in an organic solvent or an aqueous solution at elevated vapor pressure and above 300 °C, respectively. Since the first report on the hydrothermal production of small quartz crystals in 1845, diverse NMs have been created in this way (Fig. 9(a)). Early in the twenty-first century witnessed a boom in the wet chemical production of pure 2DMs such as graphene and MoS2, and recently doped 2DMs, NCs, and their heterostructures have also been similarly produced by adding a mixture of precursors and dopants in a solvent to improve the material characteristics for particular devices. For instance, the addition of boron (B) dopants significantly improved the hydrogen evolution process in GO.267
 Fig. 9  (a) Timelines of wet chemical synthesis methods.178 (b–e) TEM and AFM investigations of GO and rGO NMs. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 268. Copyright (2020), Elsevier. 

3.4.1. Solvothermal reduction method. 
In addition to thermal reduction, another straightforward way to prepare rGO-based NMs is solvothermal synthesis reaction. Specifically, a GO dispersion is put in autoclave reactor, and the applied temperature of the system is increased beyond the boiling point of the solvent. In the autoclave, de-oxidation of GO is facilitated by high temperature and autogenous pressure. Some high-boiling point solvents are used for the solvothermal synthesis of rGO, such as N,N-dimethylformamide (DMF)269 and N-methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP).270 Yuan et al.190 tested dispersed GO in C3H6O with moderate ultrasonication for 1 h to evaluate the solvothermal synthesis reduction method, followed by the addition of NaClO. The above-mentioned mixture was transferred to an autoclave reactor made of stainless steel, sealed, and heated for 2 h at about 120 °C. Subsequently, the autoclave was cooled to RT before being cleaned with water and tetrahydrofuran and dried for 24 h in an oven at 80 °C. Consequently, the reduction process removed the bulk of the oxygen functional groups in GO, resulting in the creation of BL graphene. This approach is straightforward, cost-effective, and appropriate for manufacturing graphene on a large scale, according to the authors. The TEM and AFM images of GO and rGO are shown in Fig. 9(b)–(e). Although the height profile from AFM showed that the average thickness of the GO and rGO NSs was about 0.980 nm and 1.101 nm, respectively, the wrinkles in the TEM results showed that the NSs are relatively thin.271,272


3.4.2. Hydrothermal reduction technique. 
Graphene NMs are produced during hydrothermal synthesis by heating the epoxy rings and shattering them.273 To produce rGO, the temperature is typically in the range of 120–180 °C for 1–18 h.274 Oxygen-containing groups are eliminated by the overheated supercritical water acting as a reducing agent, which results in the recovery of the aromatic lattice structure of carbon.275,276 A further factor that requires attention is the pH of the aqueous media. Large graphitic domains are maintained when the procedure is performed at pH = 11, resulting in a stable suspension of graphene with a low content of defects. Alternatively, the aggregation of graphene and even other graphitic nanostructures typically occurs in an acidic environment (pH = 3). In addition, several defects and lower lateral sizes are created. Water acts as a source of H+ for OH protonation during the acid-catalyzed dehydration of alcohol.277 In comparison to other chemical reduction techniques, hydrothermal synthesis is a simpler setup, which requires only an autoclave, and produces intrinsically pure and environmentally friendly products. Also, it produces fewer defects because the process recovers the conjugation after dehydration given that it occurs in a closed system at a relatively elevated temperature and P.278 However, the limitation of this strategy is that it is time-consuming.
Wet chemical synthesis is suitable to control the surface shape, crystallite size, and dopants in 2DMs for catalysis, renewable energy production/storage, and chemical/biological sensors. For different types of 2DMs and their NCs, the reaction temperature, precursors, and additives can be tuned, allowing for their practically limitless mass manufacture. It is also feasible to directly synthesize 2DMs on a specific substrate, although this process requires a little more time of up to a few days. Due to the limited durability of apparatus under harsh conditions, such as high P and corrosive chemicals, the growth temperature is frequently restricted to below 300 °C. It should be noted that although the bottom-up synthesis frequently results in low-quality, imperfect 2DMs, it is still viable to use these materials for catalytic purposes.

3.5. Chemical vapour deposition (CVD) technique
For the significant production of 2DMs in large quantities, there are 4 typical bottom-up synthesis methods. The first is the chemical vapour deposition (CVD) technique. The other three bottom-up thin-film deposition methods including pulsed laser deposition (PLD), atomic layer deposition (ALD), and molecular beam epitaxy (MBE) have also been studied for the fabrication large-size 2D films. CVD is mainly suitable for industry considering the uniformity and crystallinity of 2D NM-based thin films with high yield, low cost, and scalability. The other two methods being researched for the mass manufacturing of for 2DMs are bottom-up wet chemical synthesis and top-down liquid exfoliation.
On a metal surface at a high temperature, the CVD technique may create high-quality, large-area continuous graphene-NM-based thin films.279 This growth approach allows the precise control of the thickness of graphene NMs, although it has a significant energy and financial cost. A significant proportion of the expenditure is the fabrication of the metal substrate and the power needed for heat treatment. In the case of CVD synthesis method-based manufacturing of graphene-NM thin films with significant batch production and continuous growth, the batch-to-batch (B2B)280 and roll-to-roll (R2R) techniques are envisioned as two industrially significant techniques. Two commercial types of graphene-based NM products, i.e., inch-sized graphene wafers and continuous graphene films, are defined by these two commercially viable growth techniques. Between them, inch-scale graphene wafers exhibit greater quality and homogenous graphene growth, providing benefits in terms of scalability and price. Consequently, continuous graphene films can be employed to create transparent conductive films (TCFs) and graphene wafers to fabricate electronic devices. CVD uses the chemical reactions of gas-phase precursors on the surface of a heated substrate to fabricate various-sized thin films. Initially, the CVD technique was applied for the synthesis of Si materials, but later it was extended to 2DMs, for instance, graphene thin films. The most suitable technique for creating high-quality graphene-based thin films on a large scale is CVD.281 The basic CVD method involves the adsorption of reactant gases on the heated substrate or gas-phase reactions to form intermediate reactants, which also adsorb on the substrate. Gas species (such as hydrogen and Ar) are fed into the reactor during the CVD process, which pass through the hot zone, where hydrocarbon precursors (such as CH4) decompose on metal substrates (such as Cu and Ni) to create SL and FL graphene. After the initial or intermediate reactants diffuse to the substrate surface, heterogeneous reactions at the solid-gas interface result in the formation of thin films. The metal substrate serves as an electrocatalyst by reducing the energy needed for breakdown and by establishing the process of graphene deposition, which has a significant impact on the graphene quality.282 The control of the variables in the CVD method for the production of 2D graphene materials, for instance, electrocatalysts is crucial, including the precursors, gas flow rate, applied pressure/temperature, and time (Fig. 10(a)).
 Fig. 10  (a) Timelines of milestones for CVD.178 (b–e) 4H-SiC and 6H-SiC layer stacking, Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 283 Copyright (1990), IOP publishing. 
The four fundamental steps in the CVD technique, which is a complex heterogeneous catalysis scheme, are as follows:
(1) Gas precursor adsorption and catalytic degradation.
(2) Diffusion of decomposed carbon species and dissolving into the bulk metal, both on the surface and in it.
(3) Dissolved carbon atoms accumulate on the metal surface.
(4) Surface growth and nucleation of graphene (Fig. 10(b)).284
There are several instances of the wafer-scale CVD creation of thin films (Fig. 10(b) and (c)). The most popular substrates, besides transition metals (TMs) such as Pt, Co, Ru, Au, and Rh, and alloys such as Cu–Ni and Au–Ni, are Ni and Cu.179 For instance, wafer-scale single-crystal SL graphene was produced using single-crystal substrates such as H-Ge (110) and Cu (111).292,293 Wafer-scale polycrystalline SL and ML graphene thin films have also been effectively produced by the CVD technique on polycrystalline Cu and Ni foils since 2009.294 The wafer-scale fabrication of single crystals based on ML graphene thin films was also accomplished on Si–Cu alloys.295 The CVD procedure with Ni films is presented in Table 7, whereas the conditions for the CVD process with Cu films are presented in Table 8.282 Because Ni has an unfilled d shell and exhibits a certain level of carbon solubility, forming 2D graphene NMs, it has distinct properties compared to Cu (Fig. 10(d)). Alternatively, Cu has weak affinity to carbon and is a filled shell metal, which impedes both the dissolution and subsequent segregation stages (Fig. 10(e)). Then, graphene can be produced by deriving carbon atoms from the hydrocarbon that directly diffuses on the metal substrate (Table 8). S. K. Bae et al.280 invented the R2R CVD approach given that the current method of transferring graphene to metal substrates using etchants is costly, creates hazardous waste, and results in graphene having structural flaws. However, its wider use such as electrical and optoelectronic applications still needs to be improved.
