For high-quality thin film deposition, all CVD reactors must precisely regulate the delivery of the gas reactants to a vacuum-sealed chamber and the evacuation of unreacted species and gaseous by-products. The temperature must be high enough, or more energy must be added, for the gas-phase processes to take place (for example, with plasma). Given that different substrate catalytic capacities result in diverse outcomes, substrate selection and pre-treatment are essential for 2DMs to enhance thin film development. In a non-reactive environment, precursors such as hydrocarbons for graphene and oxide powders for TMDs react to form islands that develop and coalesce into a continuous thin film on the selected substrate. The temperature, concentration of active species, surface characteristics of the substrate, and other factors during the nucleation stage affect the nucleation density and nuclei orientation, which dictate the quality of the thin film. Final cooling promotes thin film growth and additional microstructural evolution of the film through wrinkling and folding.
Under atmospheric-pressure or low-pressure, CVD results in the formation of very good 2D films, and by enlarging the chamber, the size of the film can be readily scaled up. However, its industrialization may be hindered due to the requirement of high reaction temperatures (over 500 °C). Currently, there are insufficient precursors for the preparation of a broad range of 2DMs, including graphene, h-BN, and TMDs. The lack of dangling bonds on the surface of 2D vdWs NMs makes epitaxial growth challenging; hence, this method has limited control over the number of produced layers, which may be its biggest technical hurdle. To date, it is still difficult to manage the film stoichiometry and generate high-quality large-area TMD thin films. One type of CVD method (metal-organic CVD) creates consistent wafer-scale, atomically thin films of TMDs, but it has complicated growth parameters and requires numerous precursors. In general, high temperature and high vacuum conditions are required for CVD reactors for the preparation of 2DMs to fracture the precursors into reactive products that can easily form thin films but the residual precursors can seriously pollute the reaction chamber. Another difficulty is transferring films successfully from the target substrate for their growth. High-throughput in situ optical monitoring technologies including confocal laser scanning microscopy and Raman, wide-field Raman, and microwave spectroscopy can be used to improve the CVD procedure.
The crystallinity and freshness of the substrate surface affect the repeatability and dependability of CVD-grown thin films. For instance, to enhance the quality of graphene thin films, copper (Cu) foil is often electrochemically processed and re-crystallized. The homogeneity of the temperature and gas flow inside the CVD reaction chamber is also important for the synthesis of homogenous 2DMs. In the case of dependability, post-CVD treatments are also crucial. For instance, doping of graphene is desirable for real-world applications, but the instability and in-homogeneity of the doping compromise the repeatability. Thus, to advance CVD, new precursors must be developed, the deposition temperature must be lowered, and high-quality, large-scale thin films besides graphene must be consistently grown on a range of substrates for industrial use.
With the advantages and disadvantages of various manufacturing techniques, the graphene-based NM market has seen a significant rise in worldwide fabrication capacity in all three product groups over the past 10 years, going beyond research and development and into global commercialization. Particularly, recent improvements in manufacturing facilities and quality enhancement have led to CVD method-based graphene-based NMs occupying a larger share of the market. Chinese manufacturers, who have consistently increased their production capacity in recent years, have become the largest provider globally. The second-largest country, the USA, offers steadily rising manufacturing capacity. Even with the shortage of graphite, Europe is still in third position. Unfortunately, difficulties arise in the excessive manufacturing of graphene-based NMs, including pervasive inconsistent performances and characteristics. Thus, the road to optimum industrial production is still lengthy. Using China's graphene industry as an example, its capacity for economically scalable manufacturing is sufficient with the production volume of 2000 tonnes per year of graphene-NM powders and 3.5 million-m2 per year of graphene-NM-based thin films. However, in reality, the quality of graphene-NM-based products varies greatly given that it is influenced by the 2D graphene-NM supply, production processes, and fabrication conditions. There are still glaring discrepancies in the NF size and layer count of commercially available graphene-based NM powders, with significant variations in their defect density and impurity concentration. Due to these significant differences in quality, publications on the use of graphene-based NM powders have been overstated, with concerns raised about their inconsistent performance. Similar to CVD-based-synthesized graphene-NM-based films, graphene goods from various producers have drastically varying conductivities. The consistency of CVD graphene NSs varies from B2B.
For industrial-scale applications, the method for mass-producing graphene NMs must be able to deliver the necessary numbers, while guaranteeing consistent quality. However, the current methods for the production of graphene NMs are still inefficient and not cost-efficient.301,302 According to the production techniques, the worldwide market for graphene and its derivatives can be classified into three main product categories, i.e., graphene NFs (non-oxidized), GO or rGO, and graphene films. Graphene NM products show notable variations in impurities, size/thickness uniformity, yield, lateral size, defect density, manufacturing expenditure, marketplace portion, and potential device applications.
4. Graphene-based nanocomposite materials
Numerous graphene NMs can possibly be utilized to create NCs with remarkable qualities given that they feature a variety of novel characteristics.208 The corresponding graphene NM has potential to increase the conductivity and strength of bulk materials and contribute to the development of NCs with better properties. Graphene NMs combined with conductive NCs made of metals, polymers, and ceramics can withstand high temperature and pressure. Graphene-based NCs have demonstrated strong potential for numerous applications. One graphene-polymer based NC was shown to be light, flexible, and possess outstanding conductivity, while another dioxide-graphene NC was found have remarkable photo-catalytic efficiencies. There are many other possible combinations of materials that can one day be used to create various NCs, resulting in endless applications. The possible applications of graphene NCs include medical implants, technical materials for aircraft, renewable materials, and much more. However, the majority of these uses depend on the large-scale, industrial manufacture of synthetic graphene NCs in exceptionally high quality.303 The construction of complex nanoscale structures such as graphene NMs is necessary for more sophisticated applications in nanoelectronics given that they generally transmit electricity and tolerate heat better than bulk materials.
5. Perspectives and challenges in the synthesis of graphene toward industrialization
The aforementioned processes make it possible to produce 2DMs, especially graphene in large quantities, although significant advancements are still needed for some particular applications. Wafer-scale layer control has enabled the practical synthesis of single-crystal graphene NM-based films. Controlling the thickness of these materials is crucial for high-performance electronics and tunneling barriers. Flexible heterostructures with exceptional physical features can be created in 2D systems by mixing adjustable B.G semiconductors with metals and insulators. However, although numerous planar and vertical heterostructures have been created thus far, they are all micron-scale level.304,305 Generally, it is still difficult to generate different heterostructures on the wafer scale (Fig. 11(a)). Atomic saw-tooth surfaces can provide the best growth substrate for single-crystal 2DMs such as graphene and related heterostructures, but surface facet control is still difficult to achieve.
 Fig. 11  Challenges in the mass production of 2DMs. (a) Single-crystal homo/hetero-epitaxial development and wrinkle creation by thermal expansion coefficient mismatch in 2DMs and substrates, where cracking/contamination in the transfer route are the main concerns in the CVD method. (b) Inhomogeneous size and thickness of 2D NSs and low yield are drawbacks of liquid exfoliation. (c) Low durability and instability of 2DMs by defects and environmental pollution are drawbacks of wet chemical synthesis method. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 306 Copyright (2022), Springer Nature. 
Another significant problem is the development of wrinkles in 2DM-based films during their high-temperature formation, which results from the thermal expansion coefficient mismatch in the growth substrate and 2DMs. Recently, it was revealed that fold-free single-crystal graphene thin films could grow at about 750 °C;307 however, more research is needed to determine that this technique can be used for other 2DMs and low-temperature growth techniques. Due to the incompatibility of existing Si-technology with high-temperature procedures (over 400 °C), 2DM-based thin films generated by the CVD deposition method at an elevated temperature must be shifted on a target substrate. The traditional transfer method may result in major issues including 2D film folding and breaking, eventually reducing the film homogeneity and device performance. Additionally, the impurities in polymers, which are frequently included as a supporting layer for the transfer process, may result in accidental doping and high contact resistance in heterostructure devices and interfaces. Consequently, new techniques for the large-area direct growth of 2D films via CVD or sophisticated R2R transfer techniques are greatly required. The manufacturing process, including scalable methods (R2R, B2B, etc.), production capacity/expenditure, repeatability, and large-area homogeneity, should also be considered.308
For the bulk manufacture of 2DMs, wet chemical techniques such as liquid exfoliation and wet chemical production method also encounter several difficulties. For the mass manufacture of 2D NSs using liquid exfoliation, chemical vapor transport (CVT) or flux techniques are used to create pure 2D bulk materials. However, because these synthetic processes normally take place for at least a week, they reduce the manufacturing throughput, where businesses need to be able to supply these bulk materials on a greater scale. Additionally, using this approach makes it challenging to produce 2D NSs with a consistent size and thickness (Fig. 11(b)). Improved methods (such as density gradient ultra-centrifugation) are required to sort the produced NSs according to their size and thickness to overcome the above-mentioned issues. 2DMs with poor crystal quality are often produced via bottom-up chemical synthesis. The defect sites (i.e., edges) frequently act as active sites in 2D electrocatalysts, but they also cause problems including instability and low durability. Additionally, the size and thickness of the 2DMs produced by chemical synthesis are not dispersed consistently, necessitating extra attention during their synthesis. Also, the by-products routinely produced during chemical synthesis can prevent catalytic activity. Post-treatments such as thermal annealing and purification have been proposed to address these material quality and by-product problems, but a straightforward procedure without post-treatment would significantly increase productivity. The use of supercritical fluid regions may be considered as a facile way to reduce chemical use due to the environmental contamination produced by the significant number of hazardous chemical waste utilized in synthesis (Fig. 11(c)), which is another significant concern.309 Additionally, instruments for quick and accurate non-destructive characterization are crucial for assessing the wafer-scale quality and homogeneity of 2DM samples. The electrical and optical characteristics of 2D NM-based films can be analyzed using the most advanced terahertz image, i.e., phase-shift interferometry, and wide-field Raman imaging techniques, which have short acquisition times per mm2 and high spatial resolutions in the order of micrometers.310 A 12-inch wafer-scale sample still has to be extensively examined over an extended period, and thus the creation of improved characterization tools is still necessary. There is a lot of potential in the materials world for innovative 2D-NMs that have not yet been studied and their vdWs heterostructures. The industrialization and mass production of these freshly discovered 2D-NMs may benefit from artificial intelligence-based material design given that it is very hard to examine all these materials experimentally.311
6. Fuel cell technology
To obtain high electrical power densities required by a wide range of critical applications, FCs are the most promising technology. They achieve their goal by “changing the energy held in chemical bonds into electrical energy.” Specifically, FCs are electrochemical devices that transform chemical energy into electrical energy.312 Due to their exceptional energy density, high efficiency, and few pollutants released, FCs have been the subject of much study for both mobile and permanent applications that generate clean, sustainable and renewable energy. The different types of FCs include alkaline-FCs (AFCs), polymer electrolyte membrane (PEM) FCs, solid polymer electrolyte FCs (PEMFCs), direct-methanol FCs (DMFCs), phosphoric acid FCs (PAFCs), molten-carbonate FCs (MCFCs), and solid-oxide FCs (SOFCs), which are shown in Fig. 12.
 Fig. 12  (a) Main properties of graphene NMs related to FC applications18 and (b) types and main structure of FC; where oxidation takes place at the anode, while reduction occurs at the cathode. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 213 Copyright (2009), the American Chemical Society. 
In FCs, their structural arrangement results in an increase in temperature; however, it simultaneously causes an overall decline in effectiveness.313 This is the primary challenge with the many forms of FCs, which each has their own advantages, limits, and novel applications. Consequently, researchers are focusing on cutting costs, while increasing efficiency. In the next section, we summarize the basic working principle of FC devices.
6.1. Working principle and structure of fuel cell devices
The cogeneration unit of FC devices converts hydrogen-based chemical energy into heat and electrical energy.314 Generally, the hydrogen supplied at the anode is used as fuel, while the cathode provides an oxidant. The cell becomes polarized as a result of the hydrogen oxidation process (HOR) and oxygen reduction reaction (ORR), which occur on the anode and cathode, respectively.315 At an externally connected load, the current generated by the reactions is measured. The theoretically proposed PCE of FCs is 83% at standard pressure and temperature settings, while accounting for generated heat. The open circuit voltage (OCV) of about 1.23 V has been achieved under these circumstances devoid of any electrical loss. However, the commercially available devices are not very effective and have a low OCV. The decreased performance of FC devices is attributed to their variable working conditions, including temperature and pressure. Additionally, the operation voltage losses significantly diminish their electrode potential and impair their efficiency. Performance problems in FC devices are caused by a variety of dynamics of electrochemical reactions, including the rates and electrochemistry of reactions, inability of gases to reach the reaction active sites, low internal electrical currents, high electrode resistance, and crossover effect.314 Additionally, the efficiency of the cell is impacted by the Ohmic losses, concentration, and activation polarization. The above-mentioned phenomena can be analyzed using the V–I polarization plot based on the entire range of power for FC devices.316 The OCV, current density, Tafel slope, transfer coefficient, maximum power density, and efficiency are some fundamental variables used to calculate the performance of FC devices. The following equation yields the V–I polarization curve:E = Er − RT/αFln(i + iloss/i0) − RT/nFln(iL/(iL − I) − iRintwhere, Er stands for the reversible potential under STP conditions, T denotes the applied temperature, R stands for the ideal gas constant, I is the transfer coefficient, “i0” the exchange current density, “iloss” the current loss, “iL” the limiting current density, “F” is Faraday's constant, “n” is the number of e−s per hydrogen molecule, and “Rint” the internal resistance.314 For FCs to convert energy electrochemically, the gadget must be properly designed. An FC assembly integrates BPPs, membrane electrode assemblies (MEAs), current collectors, and support components.317Fig. 13(a) and (b) schematically show the structure of an MEA. The parts assembled in Fig. 13(b) provide the functions needed for electrochemical processes and are essential for the functioning of the FC.318 The primary processes in FCs include removal of waste gases and by-products, electrocatalysis for the HOR and ORR, transfer of positively charged ions through the membrane, and electron transference through outwardly attached supporting elements. Fuel and oxidant gases flow at a constant rate over the channel of the BPPs and electrodes.
 Fig. 13  (a) Simple structure of an FC device and (b) assembly of FC stack. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 319 Copyright (2019), Elsevier. 
6.2. Importance of membrane in fuel cell technology
After William Robert Grove created the first FC in 1893, or over 109 years ago, it was rarely investigated. However, in the late 1980s, FCs emerged as one of the greatest alternatives for renewable energy application. The PEM serves as the foundation of FCs given that the other parts of FCs must be constructed in accordance with the characteristics of the PEM.320 However, electrolyte conductance also plays a significant role in the power density and efficiency of FCs. The efficiency of PEMFCs is substantially impacted by the performance of the MEA. Proton conducting membranes have historically been shown to be necessary for the longevity and effectiveness of PEMFCs.321,322 Thus, to improve the performance of FCs, novel polymeric proton conducting membranes are being studied. Nafion is the focus of study, but researchers are currently exploring ways to modify it and other polymers to create optimal membranes for FCs. The proton conducting membrane of FCs must have the following properties:
(1) High proton conductivity.
(2) Chemical and electrochemical stability.
(3) Thermal and hydrolytic stability.
(4) Outstanding water uptake.
(5) Strong chemical attributes.
(6) Limited permeability to reactive species.
(7) Fuel transport resistance.
(8) Reliable mechanical strength and stability.
(9) Extremely robust to make fast electrode kinetics possible.
(10) Adaptable to various fuels and inexpensive.
Additionally, the performance of FCs is significantly impacted by a membrane thickness and water management. In FC devices, the membrane primarily serves two purposes as follows:
(1) It may act as an electrolyte in the cathode and the anode for ionic conduction, but it is an electrical insulator and
(2) it may separate the two reactant gases.
The advancements in membrane technology are now strongly related to the performance of FC devices. However, only a few membranes are currently commercially available and have shown to be beneficial in FC systems, including perfluorinated ionomer (PFI) membranes, Dow membranes, and Nafion (DuPont de Nemours). Dow membranes function noticeably better than Nafion-117, and are also more affordable. However, Dow membranes have several major issues, including an inappropriate water balance and excessive CH4O permeability, and they cost roughly 800 USD. Furthermore, there are significant issues with the typical membranes related to temperature and the need for additional equipment to solve safety issues during their preparation and use. The conductivity of the Nafion membrane decreases at a rate of around 10 times in the temperature range of 600 °C to 800 °C. Corrosive gases and hazardous intermediates develop at high temperature exceeding 150 °C, increasing the safety issues. These issues related to the membranes may restrict the ability of FCs to be recycled and increase the risk of auto accidents.323 Therefore, PFI membranes are not suitable for commercialization in FCs because of their significant disadvantages and high cost. Consequently, finding less expensive polymers is crucial for the fabrication of decent membranes for FCs.
The features of membranes still require the development of their proton conductivity, CH4O crossover reduction, and thermal stability. Thus, to achieve this, new inexpensive polymeric membranes may be the best option. According to Smitha et al.,323 15 membranes out of 60 options can take the place of traditional Nafion-based membranes. However, without functionalizing or altering the membrane, it is difficult to get the necessary qualities. The plain poly(ether ether ketone) was first altered by Hasani-Sadrabadi et al.324 by adding sulfonated groups, which increased the negative functionalities and increased the proton conductivity of the polymer. Then, the sulfonated polyether ether ketone (SPEEK) membrane was functionalized by adding a customized montmorillonite (MMT) for improved OCV and decreased CH4O uptake. In comparison to Nafion-117 and plain SPEEK membrane, the functionalized SPEEK/MMT-membrane performed better, according to the data. Subsequently, Li et al.325 added phenylidane to a polymer matrix made of polybenzimidazole (PBI), which improved the proton conduction of an electrolyte membrane used in high-temperature FCs. Similar to how Nafion has been altered in other experiments in various ways to improve its performance, Hasanabadi et al.326 synthesized and used Fe2O3 NPs to develop the performance of a Nafion membrane, in contrast to Makinouchi et al.,327 who employed SPI to change the Nafion membrane. Additionally, silica/silicotungstic acid (SiO2/SiWA) inorganic NCs328 and Pd–Si nano-fibers329 were added to Nafion to reduce the issue of CH4O crossover without compromising its proton conductivity in FCs. Similarly, several other studies have demonstrated the significance of membrane functionalization to enhance the performance of FCs. Currently, there is a big market for inorganic fillers for membrane modification in the energy sector.
Among the nano-sized CMs, CNTs have been quite successful in changing the membrane in FCs. However, since the introduction of graphene, the aim of researchers has changed. Graphene has made a substantial contribution to improve the performance of FCs, particularly at the cathode and in separation membranes. Recent research has shown that adding GO-based NMs to PEMs has a positive impact. The fundamental feature of GO is that it is a soft substance with an amphiphilic nature. It possesses exceptional chemical and physical properties with a high SSA. Additionally, GO has interfacial groups such as epoxy, carboxylic acid, and hydroxyl that contain oxygen. Acidic groups and hydrophilic sites such as carboxylic acid and epoxy oxygen may enhance the hopping of electrons and the GO hydration.330 Due to the interactions in the intermolecular hydrogen bond and membrane structure, GO can also boost the proton conductivities of GO-NM-based membranes.331 To increase the utilization of graphene NMs as desirable fillers and achieve the required alterations in PEMs, GO-based NSs have been functionalized. Although the uniform distribution of GO NPs is somewhat challenging in some organic solvents because of the higher hydrogen bonding, which causes agglomeration,332 functionalized GO (F-GO) is easily dispersed in diverse organic solvents, enhancing the distribution of the nano-fillers in polymers.333 Additionally, the functionalization of GO can enhance the membrane structure for improved proton conductivity and water retention.334
In the manufacture of membranes for FCs, the significance of graphene has previously been demonstrated. In addition to being employed as a polymer matrix additive or filler, graphene may be present as papers or NSs, which must be assembled during the production of the membrane electrode assembly (MEA).335 For energy devices and electrochemical applications, the Hummers' approach is typically favored; however, other methods and their modifications can be employed similarly to that outlined below. Tateishi et al.336 reported that GO NMs exhibit excellent proton conductivity regardless of having low temperature and relative humidity (RH). The main oxygen-containing functional groups in GO, epoxide, act as proton transfer sites after binding with water molecules, even at low RH and RT. The results demonstrated that the proton conductivities are between 105 and 104 S cm−1 at the temperature of 250 °C and RH of 10–20%. GO-based NMs can be employed in a variety of uses, e.g., electrochemical and battery-related ones. Commercial FCs have greater proton conductivity than thick GO paper when the Nafion membrane is completely wet at a high RH (100%). However, given that the thickness of GO films has a significant impact on their proton conductivity, thinner GO films can compete with Nafion membranes. The performance of a GO FC with Pt/C electrodes is shown in Fig. 14, where it is clear that it performed better than the Nafion membrane FC with Pt/C electrodes.
 Fig. 14  Cell voltage vs. current/power density curves for MEA of different materials. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 337 Copyright (1996), The Royal Society of Chemistry. 
6.3. Various classifications of fuel cell technology
Typically, the working temperature, fuels, oxidants, and electrolyte type are characterized for FCs. Hydrogen–oxygen and ammonia-air FCs are examples of devices based on a particular fuel and oxidant, whereas temperature-dependent FCs are specified for their low/high working temperature, respectively. AFCs, PAFCs, MCFCs, PEMFCs, DMFCs, and SOFCs are various types of FCs based on different electrolytes, according to the most common foundation for the categorization of FCs. The type of electrocatalyst used, operating temperature, fuel compatibility, and other operational factors are all determined by the device type, together with the electrochemistry and kinetics of processes occurring in the cell. In Table 9, we focus on hydrocarbon FCs, in which liquid hydrocarbons, such as methanol or ethanol, are fuels and polymer electrolyte membrane (PEM, primarily Nafion®) and the electrolyte. The comparative study of various FCs based on various electrolytes is summarized in Table 10.

6.3.1. Alkaline fuel cells (AFCs). 
The electrolyte membrane in AFCs is an alkaline electrolyte, for instance a concentrated solution (40%) of KOH. Negative ions (OH−) are delivered to the anode through the electrolyte in accordance with the basic principle of AFCs, combining with hydrogen to form water and generating negatively charged electrons. Over the years, AFCs have attracted significant interest. The two most well-known uses of AFCs are the space shuttle and Apollo programs. KOH solution is present in AFCs as an electrolyte absorbed in a polymer matrix (e.g., asbestos). For electrocatalysis over porous electrodes to accelerate the response, a range of metals including precious metals, e.g., Ni, Pd, and Pt, and associated oxides have been explored.347 The operating range for AFCs is in the range of 100–250 °C, despite the fact that a high performance of about 60% is attained for AFCs at 200 °C. As a result of the alkaline conditions that improve the electrode reaction kinetics and give nonprecious metal electrocatalysts options, AFCs are thought to be superior to PEMFCs.348
Another important part of AFCs is the anion exchange membrane (AEM), which supports the electrocatalysts, separates the fuel and oxidant, and enhances the transport of anions.349,350 Therefore, the creation of the necessary AEM with acceptable mechanical and conductivity characteristics is crucial for the application of AFCs. Relevantly, the characteristics of numerous polymeric membranes for FC application have been noticeably enhanced by the integration of inorganic fillers in the polymer matrix. The graphene-modified polyvinyl alcohol (PVA/GO) NC-based membrane created by Ye et al.351 served as an electrolyte for AFCs. They created GO using a modified Hummers' technique, dripping GO in PVA solution before the addition of hydrazine. The plastic plate with the necessary thickness was cast using the viscous solution, and the cast PVA/GO membrane was then submerged in a concentrated KOH solution. According to their findings, graphene NM enhanced the ionic transport by forming continuous and well-connected ionic channels in the membrane structure. With 0.7 wt% graphene NM loading, the ionic conductivity of the GO-filled membrane also increased by 126%, while the CH4O permeability decreased by 55%. Upon the addition of GO, the power density and tensile strength also increased dramatically by 148% and 73%, respectively.351 Similarly, an AAEM for AAEM-FCs was prepared by modifying ML GO paper with KOH (GO-KOH); however, this membrane can also work in AFCs.352 The fabrication of the AAEM using the traditional filtering technique produced materials with an elevated tensile strength of 24.5 MPa and high swelling ratio. The GO-KOH based membrane exhibited decreased hydrogen gas permeability, highest anionic conductivity of about 6.1 mS cm−1 at 70 °C, and ion exchange capacity (IEC) of 6.1 mmol g−1, where this improved FC performance is comparable to that of conventional membranes.352
A graphene-NM-based membrane for AFCs was also created by Liu et al.353 using a different concept. They initially created quaternized graphene (QG) from graphene that had been functionalized by APTES and added to CMPSU. The various QG/CMPSU NC-based membranes were created by quaternization and alkalinization. The aim of this study was to examine how graphene NMs affected the characteristics of NC-based membranes. With 0.5 wt% and 0.25 wt% of QGs, a 4-fold increase in bicarbonate conductivity and 3-fold increase in tensile strength were observed, respectively. The linked transfer channel creation afforded by 2D graphene was believed to be the cause of the increased performance of the QG-filled membranes.
Graphene-NM-added membranes were also discovered to exhibit superior thermal characteristics, morphologies, oxide stability, alkaline resistance, swelling ratios, and water uptake to a virgin QPSU membrane.353 Consequently, the addition of graphene NMs has been shown to be a simple method for creating AEMs for FCs. The extensive description of the effectiveness of graphene-based NMs in membranes has demonstrated their potential in MFCs and AFCs. The final graphene-filled membranes have improved proton conductivity, durability, kinetics, and water retention ability, and less bio-fouling and oxygen crossover. Only a few polymer matrices were altered using graphene to create NC-based membranes for both FCs, but their modification has shown that graphene can be a potential material for PEMs in future FCs. The primary benefit of AFCs is their strong ORR activity, whereas their disadvantage is that they are susceptible to CO2. Actually, the very low amount of CO2 in the working environment affects the cell functions by reacting with KOH, therefore increasing the resistance.354 Thus, hydrogen and oxygen must be purified or pure reactant gases must be applied to prevent this. The first man-made spacecraft, such as the Apollo and Shuttle Orbiter vehicles, are well-known examples of the use of AFCs.


6.3.2. Proton exchange membrane fuel cells (PEMFCs). 
FCs produce clean, efficient energy without CO2 pollution and have high energy conversion efficiency. Based on their availability, benefits, and some practical drawbacks, the application of FCs is enhanced in different industries. PEMFCs have been shown to be superior in some aspects among the FC family members because of their low operating temperature, high power density, and superior proton conductivity. Consequently, they can be viewed as a clean power source of the future for a variety of devices. Fig. 15 depicts an overview of the gradual advancements required for PEMFCs to satisfy future high-power-density necessities, together with a description of their operating principle. Based on their specific components, the present technological status and widely recognized possibilities of PEMFCs are listed. A typical PEMFC consists of an MEA and BPPs, with the MEA consisting of a gas diffusion layer (GDL) with a microporous layer (MPL), a catalyst layer (CL), and a PEM similar to regular FCs. In PEMFCs, generally a PEM made of a solid polymer electrolyte is employed with the benchmark Pt or recently by some modified electrodes with other NMs. The electrolyte is typically a polymer based on perfluorosulfonated acid (PFSA), which conducts protons. The benchmark traditional Pt/C (20 wt%) paste is employed as the electrocatalyst for pure hydrogen fuel and Pt-Ru alloy for fuel contaminated with CO2. The GDL is a layer of carbon paper, which plays several roles in gas transport, mechanical support, and electrical connection. It is generally linked to another layer composed of carbon black and polytetrafluoroethylene (PTFE), called MPL, which aids in the elimination of electrochemically generated water. The CL is the location of the electrochemical HOR and ORR, which take place via a number of linked physiochemical methods. Because of its outstanding activity and stability in an electrochemical environment, Pt-loaded carbon finely distributed with ionomer is the most commonly employed catalyst. However, because of the high price and limited resources of the Pt-group-metal (PGM), reducing its loading is an enduring objective for the development of CLs. The PEM, which serves as a proton conductor, electrical insulator, and gas separator, is the heart of the PEMFC.19 In addition to the intended electrical output, electrochemical processes create water and heat, which impair the functioning of PEMFCs.
 Fig. 15  Summary of progress in PEMFCs for future high-power-density requirements and a scheme of their working principle, Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 355. Copyright (2022), the American Chemical Society. 
To achieve a good trade-off among enough PEM hydration, unobstructed reactant supply, high catalytic activity, and suitable component lifespan, it is critical to maintain the optimal water content and a reasonable operating temperature through water and thermal control. To fulfil the increased power density and durability needs in the future, better temperature and humidity range tolerances, and suitable mechanical strength are required. BP is a rigid support structure in the PEMFC stack, which is responsible for current collection and heat dissipation, and used for the fabrication of reactant and coolant flow fields. However, BP accounts for a significant amount of the stack volume and cost. Two BPPs made of graphite and two porous carbon (P-C)-based gas diffusion electrodes are employed.
PEMFCs exhibit outstanding efficiency compared to the environment and coal combustion engine, and thus are one suitable devices to generate power in accordance with the needs of the 21st century, which are straightforward, have a fast start-up, and can work at comparatively low temperature. These FCs may function at both low and high temperature in the range of 80 °C and 200 °C.356 PEMFCs have an efficiency in the range of 40–50%. High-temperature PEMFCs have been developed, solving the drawback of being vulnerable to CO poisoning.357 In high-temperature PEMFCs, PBI and sulfonated polymers are employed as electrolytes (ether sulfone). To obtain higher-power-density and lower-cost PEMFCs, the construction of a thinner BP with superior mass-transfer capacity, electrical conductivity, thermal conductivity, and lower cost is an apparent design option. The main attributes that make PEMFCs the most widely used FCs are their straight-forward design, straightforward production, affordability, and durability.358 The following sections provide a more thorough view of the prospective expectations and probable pathways for their execution at the component level, as represented in Fig. 15.19
Here, we present the detailed structure and revolutionization of PEMFCs. The microstructure of Nafion-based PEMFC consists of a hydrophobic perfluorinated ionomer backbone and hydrophilic sulfonic acid pendant groups, and the high proton conductivity (0.1 S cm−1) can be maintained in a humid environment. The purpose of the PEM is to enable protons to flow through, while preventing the passage of other species.359 It is a semi-permeable membrane that is often constructed of an ionomer. It is important to create PEMs with the necessary characteristics, for example, high mechanical, chemical, and thermal stability and robust tolerance to electrocatalyst.360 PEMs are also used in redox flow batteries, electrolysers, and other electrochemical energy conversion/storage devices.359 Fuel crossover in two electrodes and electron transfer are both avoidable with PEMs. Perfluorosulfonic acid (PFSA) membranes, also called Nafion in the marketplace, are the most widely used PEMs due to their mechanical strength, high conductivity, superior chemical resistance, and durability.361 Ionomer membranes, as previously mentioned, are crucial in PEMFC systems. Currently, the use of perfluorinated ionomer membranes such as Flemion and Nafion is preferred in PEMFCs despite the fact that they have severe issues including high cost and high fuel crossover. However, Nafion has certain drawbacks, including high cost, the inclusion of electro-catalyst, electrolyte, shrinkage, high CH4O permeability, and dehydration at high temperature.362 Therefore, improvements in cost-effectiveness and discovery of alternative solid proton conductors have become a research focus in the PEMFC sector. Nafion still has issues including high cost, swelling, poor conductivity at high temperature and/or low humidity, permeable to CH4O, fuel crossover, and unstable mechanical strength at the working temperature.363 Thus, substitute solid proton conductors should be inexpensive, have abundant raw materials, and excellent proton conductivity, and not include any Pt.364 ZrP,21 PVA, urea copolymer NCs,365 and GO membrane are examples of Nafion-free electrolytes, which are the future of FC technology. Based on the main topic of this review, we will concentrate on graphene-based NMs.
PEMFCs, with excellent durability, elevated proton conductivity, highest power, superior thermal stability, and clearly reduced cost are presently the preference.366 Organic-inorganic hybrid NC-based membranes have recently been discovered to be a great replacement for traditional membranes. The distinctive changes in membranes using a mixture of organic and inorganic materials aid in reducing the major issues related to pure polymeric membranes.367 After inorganic fillers, the most desirable filler is graphene NMs, demonstrating potential for the modifications of the PEM for FC technology. Recently, graphene NMs have been reported as alternative PEMs to Nafion. In the future, the contribution of graphene NMs will be studied to develop the functionality of PEMs utilized in PEMFC applications. The excellent characteristics of GO include a wide working temperature range, big SSA, thermal stability, proton conductivity, strong mechanical strength, and a CH4O crossover barrier. Numerous studies have focused on GO alone or combined with Nafion and other polymers, or as a membrane that has been sulfonated. Among them, GO membranes have been given significant attention for applications as an electrolyte in PEMFCs.
One of the graphene derivatives with GO is produced by chemically functionalizing and exfoliating graphite powder, demonstrating a C/O ratio of 2.1.368 GO membranes are 2D, mixed-sp2 and mixed-sp3 hybridized graphite carbon materials functionalized with surface functional groups that contain oxygen.369 The oxygen functional groups of GO, a modified version of graphene, have a large SSA, which aids in the transport of protons via channels, and also stores water. It is interesting to note that pristine graphene was previously not a good candidate for membrane modification in FCs370 because of its high electrical conductivity, which led to a low FC voltage. However, with respect to its SSA, mechanical strength, and gas permeability, it has delivered some good results. Therefore, GO-based NMs are considered as a potential filler and electrical insulator; however, the advanced research GO NMs is extensively oxidized with a carbon to oxygen ratio of 2:1.371 Oxidation causes graphene NSs to split from the graphite stack by rupturing the bonds, resulting in the formation of sp2 graphitic domains, which are often covered by sp3 domains that contain oxygen groups.372 GO is transformed into an insulating and hydrophilic material by various oxygen groups, including epoxide, hydroxide, carbonyls, and carboxyls,373 while maintaining other qualities such as mechanical strength, SSA, and gas impermeability. Protons may be transported easily via GO and water can be absorbed more effectively.374 According to Table 11, the many characteristics of GO, including electrical insulation, gas permeability, and hydrophilicity, make it a suitable material for NC membranes (NCMs) in PEMFCs. These materials have some unique properties, including:

(1) Easy and inexpensive manufacturing process;
(2) High SSA (736.6 m2 g−1);
(3) Hydrophilicity;
(4) Good physical/chemical stability;
(5) Gas impermeability;
(6) Electronically insulating property; and
(7)
(8) The aforementioned acidic functional groups enable GO membranes to hydrate and absorb water molecules with ease.
It's interesting to note that the electrical conductivity predominates when the RH is lower than ∼40%. Numerous proton-conducting pathways can be produced by acidic functional groups and intermolecular hydrogen bonds. GO membranes get saturated with more water, which allows more water molecules to readily spin and permeate across the membrane. A water-mediated ion transport mechanism, namely the vehicle or Grotthuss method, controls the proton conductivity of GO membranes. The highest water content of GO membrane has been reported to reach 15 wt%.375 The acidic functional groups disappeared during the FC operation, and thus GO can be reduced to graphene. Therefore, GO membranes can be combined with a ceramic or polymer matrix to further enhance their electrical or mechanical characteristics.376 The MPD of 79 mW cm−2 was recently achieved by spraying a GO membrane (3 m) directly on the electrode surface.368 Overall, GOM-FCs offer the following benefits:
(1) Inexpensive and simple fabrication technique.
(2) Producing a thin, strong, solid, and homogenous membrane lacking any flaws or fractures.
(3) Possible to further increase the performance of hydrogen FCs by adding functional groups.362
GO membranes have been demonstrated to be a promising electrolyte and superior ionic conductor in the literature. Emerging technology known as hydrogen membrane FC (HMFC) gives FCs based on NC electrolytes made of hydrogen permeable alloy and perovskite proton conductor distinctive properties. These FCs do not require acid electrolyte, have high mechanical strength, little gas leakage, a Pt-free anode, and are simple to micro-fabricate, which are major benefits. However, the perovskite electrolyte has drawbacks because of its poor crystallinity, poor proton conductivity, and challenging manufacture, which is employed in traditional HMFCs. In this work, a novel HMFC employing a GO membrane as an electrolyte was proposed to address these shortcomings. The traditional perovskite electrolyte of HMFCs was replaced with a BL electrolyte membrane made of a pure GO membrane and an alloy layer that is hydrogen permeable, with a maximum hydrogen permeable thin film thickness of 40 nm. By bringing the operating temperature of the HMFC down from the intermediate temperature range to RT, this aimed to replace PEMFC. It satisfies a number of requirements, including preventing gas penetration, mechanical strength, low-temperature functioning, electronic insulation, resistance to reduction, and simplicity of micro-fabrication, by employing Pd or Ni64Zr36 hydrogen-permeable metal layers. Thus, the metal hydrogen permeable thin film on the GO membrane surface may reduce fuel gas leakage and hydrogen reduction when the FC is operating, and the hydrogen thin film also possesses catalytic activity and the capacity to transport protons, acting as the anode itself. Additionally, electrocatalysts become more effective in transferring electrons. The activation energy is decreased as a result of Pd or Ni-Zr on the surface breaking hydrogen bonds in contact with the anti-bonding orbits of the hydrogen molecules.
GO-based NMs have demonstrated potential benefits in membrane modification for PEMFCs and have outstanding compatibility with Nafion polymers. Thus, the exceptional compatibility of Nafion polymer with GO has drawn attention from numerous scientists because of their strong interfacial attraction. The side chains and backbone of Nafion can be improved by GO, improving its mechanical and thermal characteristics. Nafion has undergone several modifications as graphene has taken various forms over time. As an illustration, Ansari et al.377 changed Nafion by adding GO to the polymer matrix and discovered a considerable change in the ionic domains. In PEMFCs, NCMs made from GO and Nafion were successfully employed in 2012.378 TEM and SEM examination verified the dispersion of GO-based NMs in the Nafion polymer. The addition of GO-based NMs (4 wt%) to the Nafion polymer increased the proton conductivities of Nafion-212 and the pristine Nafion and Nafion NC by up to 0.068, 0.043, and 0.078 S cm−1, respectively at 300 °F and 100% RH. Compared to pure Nafion membranes, the greatest power density of the FC was 415 mW cm−2 when 4 wt% GO used at 30 °C and 0.390 V.378 Additionally, Nafion was studied in PEMFCs at high temperature and low RH.379 Here, polyoxometalate-coupled GO was used to modify Nafion rather than just pure GO NMs. Phosphotungstic acid (PW) and GO-based NMs were combined, and then integrated into the Nafion polymer matrix. The results demonstrated that the graphene-added membrane or Nafion/PW-mGO membrane had greater proton conductivity than the recast Nafion membrane. Additionally, the power density of the membrane with graphene-based NM was good, achieving 841 mW cm−2 with 20% RH at 800 °C,379 compared to just 210 mW cm−2 for the pure Nafion membrane. By retaining water through hydrogen bonding and lowering Ohmic resistance, the inclusion of GO aided in the transfer of protons across the membrane. Additionally, Peng et al.380 discussed the use of GO to alter the Nafion chains to create a nanohybrid membrane made of both GO and Nafion. They first created nanohybrids of GO and Nafion using an atom transfer radical addition (ATRA) reaction between the CC group of GO and C-F group of Nafion, as described using FTIR, Raman spectroscopy, and XPS. The interfacial compatibility that creates the proton conducting domains improved with the addition of GO to the Nafion chains. Consequently, the GO-based incorporated membrane had 1.6-times better proton conductivity than the recast Nafion membrane; moreover, the single cell test showed a 35–40% improvement in PEMFC performance.380
Consistent with this, Feng et al.335 modified Nafion NCMs using GO. Al-foil was utilized to cover a GO layer after GO was created from graphite NFs using a modified Hummer technique. The procedure involved adding GO to the polymer solution, followed by sonication for its dispersion. The Nafion membrane performed better once graphene was added. At 30 °C and 40% RH, the proton conductivity of the recast FC was reduced by 78.14%; however, with the addition of GO to the Nafion membrane, the reduction was closer to 68.08%. The authors claimed that the inclusion of GO may increase the ability of NCM to retain water, which may also have an impact on how the water absorption is measured (improved by 8.4%). Additionally, an F-doped GO-based NM was employed to alter the NCM for PEMFCs. The authors modified the Nafion electrolyte with F-doped GO NMs using the Hummers' approach to generate GO. With the help of a toluene and 3-mercaptopropyl trimethoxysilane (MPTMPS) solution, GO underwent special functionalization. A polymer NCM was made using the solution casting process. In comparison to the recast Nafion membrane, which had an IEC value of 0.91 meq g−1, the addition of 5 and 10 wt% of F-doped GO to the Nafion polymer matrix produced superior water uptake of 2% and 6% and IEC of 0.93 and 0.96 meq g−1, respectively. Additionally, it was discovered that at 120 °C and 30% RH, the proton conductivity was 4-times greater with the addition of F-doped GO than that with the regular recast Nafion membrane. Therefore, when the power density increased from 0.042 to 0.15 W cm−2, the PEMFC performance improved.381 To improve the performance of PBI, polyethylene oxide (PEO), sulfonated polyamide (SPI), and Nafion membranes for PEMFC applications at high temperature and low humidity, researchers are looking forward at GO and its countless forms.382,383
Another popular polymer for PEMFCs is SPEEK. SPEEK has also undergone several graphene modifications to enhance the membrane performance. Investigations into the impact of low- and high-degree GO (H-GO) oxidation on Nafion-SPEEK/GO-NCMs were conducted. The XPS study indicated that the concentration of oxygen functionality in H-GO and GO was 31.8% and 28.4%, respectively, but that GO was bigger than H-GO in size. With 0.75 wt% H-GO at 90 °C and 100% RH, the addition of graphene to the Nafion/SPEEK matrix showed outstanding proton conductivity of 322.2 mS cm−1 and power density of 621.2 mW cm−2.384 Accordingly, polydopamine-modified GO (D-GO) NSs were added to the SPEEK polymer matrix to create an anhydrous PEM.385 With an integrated SPEEK matrix, a homogeneous D-GO NS dispersion was observed. This enabled the remarkable electrostatic rearrangement of the membrane structure and NC chain packing (EA). The EA at the SPEEK/D-GO interface led to proton hopping and improved proton transfer according to the Grotthuss method, resulting in the generation of acid-based pairs. Membranes have substantially greater proton conductivities than pure polymer membranes under both hydrated and anhydrous environments. In hydrous circumstances at 120 °C, GO-modified NCMs improved the cell performance, with an increase in the highest current/power density of 47% and 38%, respectively.385 This demonstrates the ability of graphene NM to improve the performances of SPEEK membranes in FCs even at high temperature and anhydrous conditions.
PBI is an excellent compatible polymer with GO-based NMs, which was recently tailored with some high-quality inorganic fillers, including SiO2, ZrP, and TiO2, and has been found to be extremely effective for PEMs in PEMFCs.386 By incorporating F-doped GO in PBI, Chao et al.331 created high-temperature PEMs for PEMFCs. Triazole F-doped GO was first developed by Yang et al.387 They discovered that the PBI/GO-based CM had superior tensile strength and proton conductivity to pure PBI membrane. Additionally, because the triazole F-doped GO is simple to disperse in polar organic solvents, it has been found to facilitate the fabrication of homogeneous membranes. According to the authors, F-doped GO can be a promising option for creating inorganic polymer electrolytes for PEMFCs. Subsequently, Chao et al.331 produced GO/PBI (BuIPBI) and isocyanate GO (iGO)/BuIPBI NCMs for high-temperature PEMFCs using 3,3′-diaminobenzidine and 5-tert-butyl isophthalic acid. The SEM characterization verified that the PBI polymer matrix contained GO and iGO. Additionally, it demonstrated how well GO and iGO interacted with BuIPBI, supporting larger acid contents and enhancing the chemical stability of the membrane. The results revealed that the proton conductivities for the iGO/BuIPBI and GO/BuIPBI membranes at 140 °C were 0.027 and 0.016 S cm−1, respectively. In addition to these well-known polymers, GO has also been employed to modify several other polymers, demonstrating both its important contribution and versatility in functionalization. For PEMFCs, Sharma et al.388 treated GO with silica and evenly disseminated it into a matrix of poly(vinyl alcohol) (PVA) and chitosan. Following the addition of various concentrations of GO to PVA/chitosan matrix, the produced REMs were characterized. The addition of graphene caused the chemical, mechanical, and structural characteristics of the membrane to significantly improve, where the proton conductivity increased from 6.77 × 10−2 (pure PVA membrane) to 11.2 × 10−2 S cm−1 (GO-based filled membrane) at 90 °C. Additionally, the mechanical and thermal stability of the PEM also increased with the addition of GO. The resultant elastic modulus was about 21.37 MPa and the maximum stress was about 54 MPa. Consequently, inorganic fillers are being used to modify PEMs for energy applications. Furthermore, Cao et al.382 also created a graphene-based poly(ethylene oxide) (PEO)/GO-CM for low-temperature PEMFCs using the solution mixing approach. The proton in NCM was given by the partly ionized-COO– and H+ ions of the –COOH groups that are present on GO NSs. They examined the Young's modulus and tensile strength of PEO/GO-CM's and compared them to the pure PEO membrane, discovering outstanding results (Fig. 16(a)). When GO was added to PEO NCM at 60 °C, they also observed excellent proton conductivity of 0.09 S cm−1 and a power density of 53 mW cm−2. Additionally, the addition of GO to PEO polymers improves their ionic conductivity by releasing the proton from the COOH group.
 Fig. 16  (a) Stress/strain curves.389 (b) Tensile strength. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 390 Copyright (2011), Elsevier. 
To create PEMs for PEMFCs, thermally heat-treated GO and poly(2,5-benzimidazole)-grafted GO (ABPBI/GO) were added to the sulfonated poly(arylene ether sulfone) (SPAES) matrix. Graphite was chemically oxidized to create pure GO, which was then combined with 3,4-diaminobenzoic acid to create ABPBI/GO. The addition of ABPBI/GO to the SPAES matrix produced improvements in dimensional stability, mechanical strength, elongation, and Young's modulus. Consequently, the SPAES/ABPBI membranes outperformed the SPAES/GO-based CMs. Additionally, the SPAES/ABPBI/GO-based CMs had greater proton conductivity than the SPAES/GO and pure SPAES NCMs because the acid-base interactions added more proton routes to the membrane structure.391 Other fillers, including polymers, have also been functionalized using GO to improve their performance as a membrane material in FCs. For instance, Pt NPs were initiated, changed, and added to polymer matrices several times to enhance their performance because Pt NPs have greater proton conductivity and larger water content. Lee et al.383 used the Hummers' technique to create GO, and then used a microwave approach to deposit 1.8 nm Pt NPs on GO, forming (Pt-G). Due to the hydrophilic nature of GO, it was discovered that the Nafion/GO-CM had a somewhat greater water absorption than the Nafion/Pt-G-NCM. Fig. 16(b) illustrates the dramatic increase in mechanical characteristics after the addition of GO. The hydroxyl interaction in GO NPs and Nafion may be because OH functional groups are connected to both entities, but they were unable to offer a convincing explanation. Alternatively, the Nafion/Pt–graphene-based CMs did not significantly improve under various circumstances. The electrical network development by Pt was the source of the e− loss, and the inadequate performance of FC was caused by the low water retention capacity of GO.383
Similarly, Pt–graphene has been employed with Nafion, although this time SiO2 was used.392 With varying weight percentages of graphene, several Nafion/Pt–graphene/SiO2-based NCMs were created and evaluated for proton conductivity and water absorption. The results only indicated an increase in cell performance for Pt–graphene contents below 1.5 wt%. As a result of the loss of electrons from the Pt-network and excess inorganic fillers, the cell performance decreased and the proton conduction was inhibited beyond 1.5 wt% of Pt–graphene.80 The observed results also showed that the Pt–graphene concentration affected how effective SiO2 is. The Pt–graphene form of graphene has certain interesting properties that can be slightly changed for effective use in other energy applications; however, it does not function well in PEMs for PEMFCs using the disclosed approach.
Recently, Yang et al.393 modified Nafion/Pt-NP/TiO2-based NCMs for PEMFCs using GO. The addition of the GO to the Nafion polymer matrix led to a 15.3% increase in water absorption. Additionally, increased IEC and proton conductivity were seen with the inclusion of GO. Due to the blocking impact of GO NSs, excessive amounts of GO were not found to be acceptable for membrane production. The membrane could retain up to 32% free water with the inclusion of TiO2, but it increased up to 45% with the addition of GO compared to pure Nafion membrane, according to thermogravimetric data, which ultimately improved the cell performance.393 With the intention to further improve the performance of these NCMs for FCs, it is necessary to conduct studies on the effect of a decreased Pt concentration in inorganic fillers. The authors ascribed the considerable influence on water absorption to the excess quantity of Pt NPs.
Ionic liquids (ILs) are receiving increasing attention these days because of their unique characteristics. There are several polymeric membranes for PEMFCs that are ILs-based; however, we only discuss the NCMs that are GO-NM based and IL-modified. The kinetics of the electrode reaction would typically be optimized, the GO tolerance would be improved, and the excessive usage of the (Pt) catalyst would be avoided at high operating temperature (>100 °C), all of which will significantly increase the performance of PEMFCs. In contrast, the Nafion membrane loses a significant amount of water at 80 °C, making it unsuitable for use at higher temperature. Due to their stability even at high temperature, protic ILs (PILs) and polymer acid complexes have attracted interest from several scientists as alternative PEMs to Nafion. One effective polymer acid complex is the PBI/H3PO4 membrane.394 The PBI/H3PO4 membrane was mechanically sound with certain restrictions given that it has great ionic conductivity when the acid concentration is high. Consequently, as water evaporates at high temperature, the PBI/H3PO4 membrane can lose the free acid. One of the potential options to lower the acid content of the PBI membrane is the addition of appropriate solid conductors. Consequently, GO and S-doped GO were added to a PBI membrane with a low H3PO4 concentration by Xu et al.331 Additionally, NCMs based on PILs and graphene-modified IL polymer (PIL [NTFSI]-G] have been researched.370 With 0.5 wt% graphene loading at 160 °C, the (PIL [NTFSI]-G] membrane demonstrated good ionic conductivity of 7.5 × 10−3 S cm−1, which is much better than that of the sulfonated polyamide/PIL (SPI/PIL) membrane. Furthermore, the addition of graphene to the membrane improved its mechanical characteristics compared to the SPI/PIL membrane, increasing the Young's modulus by 127% and the tensile strength by 345% with just 0.9 wt% of graphene. Alternatively, they functionalized GO to change the PBI membrane for PEMFCs by using 3-aminopropyltriethoxtsilane IL material. They discovered that the addition of IL GO (IL-GO) to the PBI polymer matrix improved its proton conductivity, while the phosphoric acid loading was kept at the minimum. The ionic conductivity of the IL-GO/PBI membranes at 175 °C was 0.025 S cm−1 and 0.035 S cm−1 after repeating the units of 2.0 and 3.5,395 respectively, which was superior to that of the plain PBI membrane. Similar work was recently done for alkaline AEMFCs using IL-GO/PBI AEM. The IL-GO/PBI NCM that was produced exhibited high mechanical, thermal, and alkaline stability with increased conductivity of around 10−2 S cm−1. The developed membrane satisfies the prerequisites for AEMs and exhibits significant promise for next-generation FCs.396
In addition to this GO form, GO also exists in various functionalized forms that expand and reinforce its function as an FC membrane material. To make a sulfonated poly(ether sulfone) NCM, for instance, a combination of cis and trans mesonaphthobifluorene (MNF) form of graphene was utilized, followed by its characterization. The six conjugated phenyl rings on the MNF graphene moiety created sp2-bound carbon planar NSs. The main processes in the synthesis of graphene for the poly(ether sulfone) mesonaphthobifluorene (PMNF)-based materials were polycondensation, intra cyclization, and Friedel–Crafts reaction with FeCl3. However, H2SO4 was utilized to sulfonate MNF. The main factors used to evaluate SPMNF membrane were the proton conductivity, IEC, and water uptake. The authors disclosed the potential of graphene NMs to enhance the characteristics of the membrane and concluded by advising the use of SPMNF membranes for PEMFCs. A self-supporting graphene-based membrane for PEMFCs was also created using the vacuum filtering approach.397 In contrast, the traditional Nafion membrane was made by the authors, and both were examined before being used in a PEMFC. With respect to the water absorption, elastic modulus, swelling ratio, and tensile strength, the graphene-based membrane outperformed Nafion. Furthermore, under comparable circumstances, the graphene-based membrane outperformed the Nafion membrane with respect to proton conductivity and activation energy. Additionally, the self-supporting graphene-based membrane attained a maximum power density of 33.8 mW cm−2, which is the highest value recorded for this type of membrane. GO has several advantages as a membrane material for FCs, but it still has to be functionalized to be even better. For instance, sulfonated-GO has been shown to be an excellent membrane material for FCs. Although GO-based NMs increase the proton conductivity of NCMs, the poor performance of these membranes is caused by their poor mechanical characteristics and increased water/CH4O permeability. Therefore, the detrimental effect of polymer sulfonation, i.e., loss of mechanical strength, can be minimized by the insertion of an inorganic group with enhanced carbon nanostructures, such as sulfonated-GO. The sulfonated-GO boosts the mechanical strength, while decreasing the CH4O permeability given that it creates a more winding channel for the fuel. Numerous studies investigated how the incorporation of sulfonated-GO affects polymeric NCMs, as briefly explained below. Facile distillation polymerization was employed by Liu et al.398 to create sulfonated-GO NSs and introduce them in the chitosan (CS) matrix to create a nanohybrid membrane. Investigations were done on the membrane structure and characteristics. Due to the electrostatic attraction between the –NH2 of CS and –SO3H of sulfonated-GO, which reduced the mobility of the CS chains, the GO-filled membranes demonstrated greater mechanical and thermal stabilities than the CS pure membrane. Additionally, the structural stability of the sulfonated-GO-filled membranes was strengthened due to the decrease in their swelling area as a result of the inhibition of the mobility of the CS chains. The inclusion of sulfonated-GO creates proton-hoping sites and offers channels for proton transfer, which increase the proton conductivity in both the hydrated and anhydrous states. In comparison to the pure CS membrane, the addition of 2 wt% sulfonated-GO increased the anhydrous conductivity by 90.7% and hydrated conductivity by 122.5%.398
Kumar et al.399 also modified a SPEEK membrane for PEMFCs using sulfonated-GO. The channels for proton transfer were created by dispersing the sulfonic acid in the SPEEK structure after it was utilized to functionalize GO. By using FTIR and SEM to analyze the membranes, it was possible to see how F-GO and SPEEK interacted chemically and how sulfonated-GO was evenly distributed throughout the polymer matrix. When sulfonated-GO was added at 30% RH and 800 °C, the proton conductivity of the SPEEK-controlled membrane increased to 0.055 S cm−1. Additionally, the sulfonated-GO-filled membranes exhibited increased power density (378 W cm−2) compared to the pure SPEEK membrane, demonstrating the potential of graphene to improve the performance of PEMFCs. GO was functionalized to form sulfonated-GO and its performance in a PFSA-based electrolyte, for example, Nafion-115, was assessed in another work by the same authors. Initially, they vacuum-filtered a colloidal solution of sulfonated-GO on the membrane before applying it layer by layer. Through-plane and in-plane measurements, the proton conductivity of the membrane was determined to be 0.012 and 0.04 S cm−1, respectively. The IEC was increased with the addition of sulfonated-GO from 0.93 to 1.1 meq g−1. Although the OCV was just 0.72 V, the power density of the membrane was excellent, at 113 mW cm−2 at 0.39 V.374 It was found that gas crossover, not the graphene content was responsible for the decrease in OCV value. In another investigation, sulfonated-GO was combined with a PBI membrane for PEMFCs. Sulfonated-GO was integrated into the PBI polymer matrix by Xu et al.400 to change the PBI polymeric membrane. Phosphoric acid was used to create PBI/sulfonated-GO membranes for high-temperature PEMFCs to improve their proton conductivity. Following the evaluation of the PBI/GO and PBI/sulfonated-GO membranes using XRD, the intercalation of the PBI molecules in the sulfonated-GO layer was verified.
Reduced levels of acid loading resulted in less free acid, which prevented conductivity loss and water loss. At 0% humidity and 175 °C, the addition of GO and sulfonated-GO NMs to the PBI matrix improved the ionic conductivity to 0.027 S cm−1 and 0.052 S cm−1, respectively. With a power density of 600 mW cm−2 at 175 °C, the performance of the FC was similarly excellent.401 The PEMs utilized in PEMFCs have improved characteristics as a result of the addition of graphene. Compared to pure membranes, the graphene-loaded membranes showed better chemical and mechanical stability. Additionally, the improved membrane conductivity, electrical resistance, and water retention were ascribed to the addition of graphene. It also reduced the activation energy and CH4O permeability. According to Fig. 17, GO is compatible with the majority of polymers and solvents used to create NCMs for FC applications. However, sulfonated-GO has been found to be preferable to GO due to its notable impact on the electrochemical characteristics of NCMs. The fundamental functional characteristics of ILs and Pt NPs have been greatly improved by GO, and the PEMs have been effectively adjusted for PEMFCs.
 Fig. 17  (a) PEMFC structure402,403 and (b) GO-suitable solvents for PEMs in FCs. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 404 Copyright (2018), Elsevier. 
The impact of GO on membrane alteration is suitable for use in DMFCs after PEMFCs. According to cutting-edge FCV products and global FC programs, the volume power density of a stack with end plates will approach 6 kW l−1 in the next 5 to 10 years, with the working current density and cell voltage increasing to about 3–4 A cm−2 and 0.7–0.8 V, respectively. In addition, with future advancements, an ultimate stack objective of 9 kW l−1 may be realized, with the cell operating points pushed to even higher levels, for instance, 4–5 A cm−2 and 0.8–0.9 V, respectively. The mathematical translation in two PEMFC performance indices (volume power density of stack, expressed in kW l−1, and current density of active area, expressed in A cm−2) is based on public domain data and calculation of the number of cells and end plate thickness. Unless otherwise noted, herein, the volume power density refers to that of a stack with end plates. To achieve the necessary significant increase in the PEMFC stack power density, complete knowledge of the existing problems and potential including all PEMFC components is required, which is the goal of this perspective.19


6.3.3. Direct methanol fuel cells (DMFCs). 
DMFCs are a form of PEMFCs, which are considered as one of the greatest future power-generation system alternatives due to their easy function and design with excellent energy efficiency (Fig. 18(a)). The PEM is a critical component of DMFCs, serving as a barrier to prevent direct contact between the oxidant and fuel and proton-conducting medium. One of the major hurdles in modern DMFCs is the creation of novel proton-conductive membranes. Polymer membranes must have good thermal, mechanical, and chemical stability, with low cost, low permeability to reactants, accessibility, and strong proton conduction.405 Previously, researchers employed the commercially available DuPont Nafion membrane in DMFCs because of its strong proton conduction and outstanding mechanical and chemical stabilities. However, Nafion membranes have a few drawbacks, including a low operating temperature (about 100 °C or lower), expensive material costs, and elevated CH4O permeability. Besides Nafion, numerous polymeric polymers, for example, poly(ether ether ketone) (PEEK), PBI, and polysulfone (PSF), have been successfully employed in DMFCs as a result of their economical nature and strong thermal and mechanical stabilities at high temperature. To date, substantial research has been conducted to produce novel PEMs that can meet the current performance standards for DMFCs. Among them, graphene-based NCMs have received significant attention and found to exhibit an excellent performance in DMFCs. The incorporation of graphene in the polymer matrix improves the properties of pure polymeric membranes, which in turn improves the performance of DMFCs. Similar to PEMFCs, GO NMs have also demonstrated usefulness as membrane materials in DMFCs due to their distinctive 2D structure with flexible functionalization and strong thermal stability, meeting the PEM-recommended requirements. Compared to other inorganic materials, the presence of multiple functional groups makes them more adaptable for functionalization.
 Fig. 18  (a) DMFC structure.403 (b) Proton conductivity of sulfonated-GO/SPEEK and GO/SPEEK membranes at 80 °C.420 (c) Rheological spectra of pure Nafion solution and different sulfonated-GO loadings of sulfonated-GO/Nafion solutions. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 421 Copyright (2009), Elsevier. 
There are many studies on the effect of GO on polymer matrices in recent years. Nafion is a commonly utilized polymer for the PEM in FCs. As previously noted, GO exhibits great compatibility with Nafion; hence, GO-NM-based NCMs have been thoroughly investigated for DMFC applications. Lin et al.406 and Choi et al.,361 for example, employed GO with Nafion-115 and pure Nafion, respectively. Both studies used GO to functionalize their Nafion membranes to improve the performance of DMFCs. However, these membranes were created using distinct techniques for the same application. On the one hand, the results demonstrated that the GO-laminated dual-layer PEM had 70% lower CH4O permeability and 22% lower ionic conductivity than the recast Nafion membrane in DMFCs.406 The inclusion of GO in the polymer matrix favorably altered the transport characteristics of the ionic conductive NCM. Because of the modified ionic channels, the developed membranes maintained high ionic conductivity, while decreased CH4O crossover. Furthermore, the GO filler enhanced the interfacial contacts, which enhanced the mechanical and thermal characteristics of the Nafion/GO-CM.361 Under regulated operating conditions, the GO-NM-based NCMs significantly outperformed the Nafion-112 membranes in the DMFC. Recently, Yan et al.359 sandwiched SL graphene between two thin films of Nafion to create a new NCM for DMFCs. Their goal was to lower the CH4O permeability, while maintaining selectivity. Compared to the pure Nafion membrane, the addition of SL graphene increased the proton permeability, while decreasing the CH4O permeability by 68.6%. This concept presented an effective technique to decrease the CH4O crossover, while maintaining the high proton conductivity of the NCM, and it also enhances the performance of PEMFCs.
Wang et al.407 tailored Nafion-117 with GO for PEMs in DMFCs. To create GO NCMs, the layer-by-layer (LbL) technique was employed using PDHC. According to scientists, the LbL method provides strong membrane stability and prevents the dissolution of GO in water. Under the same conditions, the Nafion/GO NCMs had 2-times the CH4O permeability and 5-times the selectivity of the recast Nafion-117 membrane. Furthermore, the endurance duration of the Nafion/GO NCMs in Fenton's reagent was 10-times that of the recast Nafion-117 membrane. The Nafion/GO NCMs also exhibited an outstanding OCV of 0.67 V with the maximum power density of about 64.38 mW cm−2 at 200 mA cm−2 and 60 °C. Yuan et al.408 developed a CH4O blocking membrane for DMFCs using a similar LbL method. However, their method was slightly different given that they tailored GO with poly(diallyl dimethyl ammonium chloride) (PDDA) and built it on the surface of a commercially available Nafion membrane. By generating a homogeneous and dense coating on the surface of a Nafion membrane, they tested the CH4O blocking function of BLs. Compared to a regulated Nafion membrane, the PDDA/GO BL NCM reduced the CH4O permeability by 67%. Furthermore, the limiting current density of the PDDA/GO BL NCM was about one-third that of the pure Nafion membrane, with a 63% reduction in CH4O crossover. The GO-filled membrane outperformed the pure Nafion membranes in both cases and was shown to be much better for DMFC applications.
Besides Nafion, sulfonated poly(ether ether ketone) (SPEEK) is a good GO-suitable polymer for PEMs, which has been utilized numerous times with F-GO in DMFCs and proven to be great with respect to PEM properties. To reveal its potential in DMFCs, a quick description of GO/SPEEK NCM is necessary. Recently, sodium dodecylbenzenesulfonate (SDBS) was used to modify GO, and this customized filler was inserted into a SPEEK membrane for DMFC applications.409 The water uptake, IEC, CH4O permeability, and proton conductivity of the SDB-sulfonated-GO/SPEEK NCMs were compared to that of Nafion-112 and pure SPEEK membranes. The SDB-sulfonated-GO filled membranes outperformed other similar membranes in DMFC application, as predicted. Recently, SPEEK has been employed with GO NSs to create hybrid PEMs for DMFCs.410 F-GO NSs were combined with histidine molecules and their influence on the NCM crystalline structure, fractional free volume, thermal stability, cross sectional morphology, and polymer chain stiffness was investigated. The concept was to merge fundamental imidazole groups (which accept protons) from the histidine molecules with acidic –SO3H groups (which donate proton) from SPEEK to generate acid-base pairs, which facilitate the synergistic transport of protons, resulting in efficient proton channels in NCM. Consequently, the proton conductivity of the hybrid membrane increased by 30.2% compared to the pure SPEEK membrane. In addition, the inclusion of F-GO NFs in the polymer matrix was responsible for low CH4O permeability of the DMFC cell of 1.32–3.91 × 107 cm2 s−1, outstanding selectivity of 5.14 × 10−5 S scm−3, and 80.7% greater maximum power density compared to plain SPEEK. SPEEK was once again tailored with GO to create an NCM for DMFCs. Lee et al.411 produced a self-humidifying membrane (SHM) by incorporating phosphotungstic acid (PWA) and carboxyl functionalized graphene (F-Gc) in a SPEEK film. According to them, the addition of G-PWA in an adequate proportion can enhance the microstructural design of SHMs by increasing their proton conductivity and water retention. After evaluation by different techniques such as XRD, SEM, XPS, and TGA, the parameters such as water absorption, IEC, and thermal stability, were studied. The results indicated that the inclusion of G-PWA in SPEEK enhanced the characteristics of the SHM at a temperature over 600 °C and has high potential for use in DMFCs at medium temperature.
Also, other GO-based polymeric membranes for DMFCs have been described. Scientists attempted to employ certain innovative ways to improve the functionalities of GO; hence, the investigations resulted in many enhanced parameters, opening the door for future PEM adjustments. Chu et al.412 studied a novel technique for using graphene in a polymeric matrix to form NCMs. They created NCMs for the first time by combining zwitterion-coated GO (ZC-GO) with PBI for DMFCs. The existence of ZC-GO was verified by FTIR, and the PBI structure and purity were studied using proton nuclear magnetic resonance spectroscopy. The results were in favor of including ZC-GO in the PBI matrix. With respect to water absorption, swelling ratio, CH4O permeability, proton conductivity, and thermal stability, the ZC-GO-containing membranes outperformed the pure PBI membranes. Especially, the proton conductivity and CH4O permeability of the 25% ZC-GO-filled membrane were 4.12 × 10−2 S cm−1 and 1.38 × 10−7 cm−2 s−1, respectively, which were superior to that of Nafion-117 under identical circumstances. The proton-transport channels in the NCMs were ascribed to the homogeneous and uniform dispersion of the ZC-GO filler. PBI/ZC-GO-CM exhibited a good performance for DMFC application. Kim et al.413 proposed a novel way to adjust GO for DMFCs. Initially, they prepared modified GO with vinyl groups (F-GO), modified montmorillonite clay with vinyl groups (F-MMT), and modified CNT with vinyl groups using an ion exchange process with 1-[(4-ethylphenyl) methyl]-3-butyl-imidazolium chloride (F-MWNT). Following the inclusion of vinyl monomers in a solvent combination of CH4O and water, a solvent-casting approach was utilized to construct polymeric NCMs containing F-GO, F-MWNT, and F-MMT by radiation-induced polymerization.
In the DMFC application, vinyl polymer-based NCMs are employed as separators. This is a novel way to improve the performance of DMFCs using vinyl polymer-based NCMs. However, graphene has demonstrated amazing flexibility with most polymers and been identified as a good material for future membranes for FCs.
