Furthermore, for DMFC applications, a PVA-grafted GO (PVA/GO) NC was integrated in sulfonated PVA.414 The findings were adequate and served to demonstrate the potential benefits of graphene in membrane formation for DMFC applications. Furthermore, Kumar et al.,415 for the first time, used GO paper as an electrolyte. However, the permeability of CH4O was about 18.2 × 10−6 cm2 s−1, which was greater than that of Nafion-115 (3.36 × 10−6 cm2 s−1) and resulted in an excellent performance with an increased power density of DMFC. Also, the latter graphene was employed as a PEM in a DMFC.416 The membrane was made by laminating GO nano-platelets. The increased proton conductivity and linear decrease in CH4O penetration rate were ascribed to the increase in the size of the nano-platelets after evaluating the membrane transport properties. The surface mobility of CH4O and proton molecules, and the many conduction channels formed by graphene may be key causes for the improved transport characteristics. Compared to commercial Nafion membranes, the GO membrane in DMFCs demonstrated a better power density and no observed loss in OCV. This demonstrates the significance of graphene-based membranes in DMFCs. After GO, sulfonated-GO has been widely employed to increase the performance of PEMs in DMFCs. GO is a non-conductive substance, requiring the removal of oxygen functional groups and reduction of the interlayer spacing to the increase conductivity; hence, GO must be converted to rGO.
Nonetheless, GO has potential to improve the proton conductivity of the polymer matrix. Heo et al.417 created an aa-type NCM to study the influence of sulfonated-GO on SPEEK for DMFC applications. When GO was sulfonated, the amount of sulfonic groups (SO3H) increased, considerably improving the proton conductivity of NCM. Furthermore, sulfonated-GO-filled membranes showed better mechanical stability than GO-added membranes and successfully stopped the flow of water and CH4O molecules through the membrane. As shown in Fig. 18(b), added sulfonic functional groups can act as a penetration route for protons, improving the membrane proton conductivity, considerably more than adding GO NPs. However, sulfonated-GO is efficient only up to a certain degree of loading, creating negative blocking effects beyond that. Similarly, sulfonated-GO and Fe3O4 were mixed, and the solution was put in a SPEEK/PVA matrix to create an NCM for DMFCs. The varying concentrations of sulfonated-GO/Fe3O4 NSs were utilized, followed by NCM performance testing. The findings indicated that including sulfonated-GO/Fe3O4 in a SPEEK/PVA mixture can improve the proton conductivity, CH4O barrier characteristics, and mechanical stability of NCMs. The best concentration of sulfonated-GO/Fe3O4 NSs was 5 wt%, with a low permeability of 8.83 × 10−7 cm−2 s−1, elevated proton conductivity of 0.084 S cm−1 at 25 °C, high tensile strength of 51.2 MPa, and good power density of 122.7 mW cm−2 at 180 °C.418 Currently, sulfonated-GO is being utilized to functionalize chitosan biopolymers (CS) for DMFCs [103]. Essentially, chitosan has limited proton conductivity and requires further modification to work well in essential energy applications. Thus, varying sulfonated-GO loadings were employed in the chitosan matrix to improve the electrochemical characteristics of the membrane. Consequently, the permeability of the sulfonated-GO-filled membrane was reduced by 23%, while the conductivity was enhanced by 454%; hence, the selectivity increased by 650% compared to the pure chitosan membrane.419
To maximize the characteristics of NCM, sulfonated-GO was functionalized with SPEEK. Jiang et al.422 effectively formed GO using 3-mercaptopropyl trimethoxysilane and produced sulfonated organosilane F-GO (SSi-GO). SSi-GO was mixed with the SPEEK polymer, and the resulting GO-based CMs was evaluated in a DMFC. The inclusion of GO significantly improved the water absorption, IEC, and proton conductivity of the NCM. Furthermore, the optimum and regulated SSi-GO content showed approximately 17% and 38% greater power densities compared to Nafion-115 and Nafion-112 membranes, respectively. Thus, for future DMFCs, the SPEEK/SSi-GO membranes provide a low-cost, high-performance membrane alternative. Although sulfonated-GO has demonstrated extraordinary effects on membrane alteration, its properties are not restricted to this level and can be functionalized for some more effective forms. Several studies looked at the impact of novel functionalized sulfonated-GO forms on PEM changes. Recently, a sulfonated polyimide (SPI) membrane was modified for DMFCs using sulfonated propylsilane GO (SP-sulfonated-GO)423 and sulfonated imidized GO (SIGO).424 The inclusion of SP-sulfonated-GO in the SPI matrix increased its proton conductivity, thermal, chemical, and mechanical stability, and the bound content of water, which caused delayed dehydration of the membrane. In addition, the SPI/SP-sulfonated-GO NC PEM exhibited enhanced self-humidification and different acidic functional groups, which are responsible for water barrier characteristics and proton conduction, respectively. The addition of graphene increased the hydrogen bonding, resulting in organic polymer chains and linked graphene NSs, which offer a hydrophilic–hydrophobic phase separation and suitable proton-conducting channel structure, respectively. The greatest power density of the SPI/SP-sulfonated-GO CM was 75.06 mW cm−2 at 700 °C, whereas Nafion-117 had a power density of 62.40 mW cm−2.423 SP-sulfonated-GO was employed in a slightly modified version in another investigation. The same group functionalized graphene to N,N-dimethylenephosphonic acid propylsilane GO (NMP-sulfonated-GO) and distributed it in a matrix for DMFCs (N-o-sulfonic acid benzyl chitosan (NSBC)). The NSBC/NMP-sulfonated-GO CMs were evaluated for the same criteria as the SP sulfonated-GO-CM previously described. The results clearly demonstrated the potential benefits of graphene with respect to the stability (thermal, mechanical, and chemical), water retention capabilities, and proton conductivity of NCMs. The functional mechanisms of SP-sulfonated-GO, NMP-sulfonated-GO, and SIGO in polymer matrices are comparable, as detailed by the authors.425 Consequently, identical results were obtained with the integration of SIGO in the SPI matrix under similar conditions. The resulting SPI/SIGO had superior water retention capacity, increased mechanical, oxidative, and thermal stabilities, and high bound water content. Furthermore, the SPI/SIGO CM exhibited an IEC of 2.24 meq g−1, proton conductivity of 11.38 × 10−2 S cm−1, bound water content of 5.12%, and CH4O permeability of 10.52 × 10−7 cm−2 s−1. The greatest power density increased from 57.12 mW cm−2 (pristine SPI membrane) to 78.53 mW cm−2 (SIGO-filled membrane) at 700 °C using 2 M CH4O fuel. Furthermore, sulfonated-GO has excellent compatibility with Nafion polymer and is commonly utilized as an NC PEM for DMFCs. Sulfonated-GO NPs have a considerable influence on the Nafion polymer matrix, altering the electrochemical characteristics of the resulting NCM.
Chien et al.426 disseminated sulfonated-GO in the Nafion matrix to improve the structure of NCM for use in DMFC. The sulfonated-GO-filled membrane exhibited significantly improved CH4O permeability, water uptakes, swelling ratio, proton conductivity, and CH4O selectivity. Furthermore, as shown in Fig. 18(c), they observed the considerable influence of the sulfonated-GO loading rheology of the Nafion polymer solution, where the cooperative bonding interaction between the sulfonated-GO particles and Nafion polymer may be the basis for the development of membrane characteristics. Furthermore, the backbone of the Nafion/sulfonated-GO-CM had a higher relaxation transition temperature, better P tolerance, and high storage modulus. XRD also demonstrated ionic cluster shrinking, which reduced the CH4O crossover in the NCM. In 1 M and 5 M CH4O solutions, the performance of the sulfonated-GO-based NCMs with DMFCs was superior to that of the commercially available Nafion-115 membrane. Furthermore, Choi et al.427 synthesized sulfonated-GO and distributed it in a Nafion polymer matrix for DMFC use. They created a homogeneous solution of sulfonated-GO and Nafion before casting the NCM. The scientists reported that the sulfonated-GO-filled NCMs outperformed the recast Nafion membrane in PEMFCs. Functionalization of polymeric membranes is critical for achieving excellent DMFC performances, where graphene has emerged with tremendous characteristics and has become the focus for PEM modifications in energy applications. Despite the scarce literature on graphene-NM-based membranes for DMFCs, the current study is capable of demonstrating the potential benefits of GO as a membrane material. The functionalization flexibility of 2D graphene NMs can lead to significant advances in membrane development for future FCs. The research on graphene-based membranes in FCs recently has been quite successful, which will undoubtedly serve as a template for future membrane research for energy applications. Although PEMFCs and DMFCs are different energy applications, the expected features and functionalities of graphene-filled membranes in both are basically identical. In summary, the addition of GO has introduced several great alternatives to pure Nafion membranes, which has also performed well with other polymers such as SPEEK, PBI, and PVA, strengthening their capabilities as PEMs for DMFCs. Furthermore, sulfonated-GO has demonstrated great impacts on the performance of PEMs in DMFCs; additionally, various types of sulfonated-GO, for example, SP-sulfonated-GO, NMPSO, and SIGO have demonstrated good results. However, sulfonated-GO requires more attention to be functionalized more effectively with the most suitable polymers to manufacture the necessary PEMs for DMFCs. Though the analysis concentrated on PEMFCs and DMFCs, the potential benefits of graphene-based membranes are not limited and have a substantial impact on the performance of other FCs.
Several investigations have been conducted on graphene utilizing Nafion NC as a membrane. Lin and Lu406 investigated a Nafion-115 membrane laminated with well-oriented GO paper and found that this type of NCM performed better in high CH4O-concentration DMFC systems. This is because the parallel alignment of the GO paper results in the successful prevention of CH4O crossing. Lee et al.383 conducted another investigation in which two types of membranes were prepared, i.e., Nafion/GO-based NCMs and Nafion/Pt–graphene-based NCMs. The water uptake and proton conductivity of both membranes were compared, and it was found that the water absorption of the Nafion/GO-based NCMs was much larger than that of the Nafion/Pt–G-based NCMs. This is because of the hydrophilic nature of GO, whereas Pt is hydrophobic, resulting in reduced water absorption. Furthermore, the Nafion/GO-CM exhibited greater proton conductivity due to the increased GO concentration.
Besides GO, another strategy was used to change graphene by adding sulphur to make sulfonated-GO, with PEMs in DMFCs performing significantly better as a result of sulfonated-GO. The sulfonated-GO outperformed GO in mechanical stability and efficiently avoided water and CH4O passage across the membrane. In addition, sulfonated-GO has sulfonic functional groups, which act as a proton permeate channel, increasing the membrane proton conductivity rather than increasing the amount of GO particles.417 However, at a certain level of loading, the performance of sulfonated-GO NM is ineffective and causes undesired blocking effects. Nicotera et al.428 studied aligned sulfonated-GO membranes and discovered an outstanding CH4O crossover blocking effect in an orthogonal route. This is the result of the elevated operating temperature (which may exceed 130 °C), which caused the twisting of the pathways to increase, while maintaining high proton mobility. The results further indicated that the Ohmic loss in a single DMFC decreased and that the appropriate power density was achieved at high temperature. Hence, the operating range of DMFCs is extended to elevated temperature. Hung-Chung426 conducted another investigation on DMFC membranes in which Nafion-115 was produced with sulfonated-GO. It was observed that the inclusion of sulfonated-GO improved the characteristics of the membranes, notably their permeability, conductivity, and mechanical stability.


6.3.4. Molten carbonate fuel cells (MCFCs). 
As the electrolyte, MCFCs or high-temperature FC devices employ a mixture of alkali and molten carbonate salt.314 Negative ions are delivered to the anode of MCFCs through a penetrating membrane. Water and electrons are produced on the anode when electrons combine with hydrogen. Because conductive slats are generated by molten carbonates at higher working temperature (600–700 °C), MCFCs do not require reformers or precious noble metal (Pt and Ni) electrocatalysts. Self-reforming occurs when the fuel (natural or synthetic gas) reforms itself at high temperature in the FC device. Higher working temperature and corrosive electrolytes have several disadvantages, for instance, increased corrosion and reduced device life. The greatest efficiency achievable with MCFCs is between 60–80%. Currently, MCFCs are being tested for commercial applications.314


6.3.5. Solid oxide fuel cells (SOFCs). 
SOFCs are devices that transform chemical energy from hydrogen-enriched fuels into electricity. Because of their lower CO2 emissions, they are considered as a more ecologically benign energy source. In SOFCs, negative charged ions are transported to the anode by membrane penetration, creating water and electrons through interactions with hydrogen. These electrochemical conversion devices work at a temperature ranging from 600 °C to 1000 °C, with ions being conducted via oxide. SOFCs do not change the solid state, despite their high working temperature. SOFCs can get enhanced reaction rates at high operating temperature without the need of pricey metallic electrocatalysts. Furthermore, because gases are internally reformed at a higher working temperature, natural gas may be used directly as a fuel without the use of an external reformer.429 SOFCs may attain a maximum PCE of 50–60%. SOFCs use a solid and nonporous metal oxide ceramic electrolyte (e.g., yttrium oxide stabilized with zirconium oxide (YSZ)).314 BiMeVOx (Bi-bismuth, Me additive metal, V-vanadium, and OX–O2), in addition to Y2O3-stabilized ZrO2, can be utilized as an electrolyte in SOFCs. These devices are among the most well-known commercialized FC devices, with applications ranging from micro to several MW. Fig. 19(a) depicts the operating conditions of a type of FC.314 The integration of GO in electrodes has the potential to significantly lower the operating temperature of SOFCs. Khalil Ahmad et al.430 investigated the incorporation of 1 wt% and 1.5 wt% GO-based NMs concentrations in the La0.3Sr0.7Fe0.4Ti0.6O3 (LSFT) cathode material (Fig. 19(b)). The LSFT oxide material was created using the sol-gel method. The XRD technique was employed to study the crystal structure and crystallite sizes, which were 73–103 nm. The SEM analysis revealed the homogeneous and porous shape of the materials. FTIR spectroscopy was used to analyze the functional group information. Electrical conductivity was measured in air in the temperature range of 300–600 °C. The electrical conductivity increased significantly for the GO-containing LSFT cathode up to a maximum value of 7 S cm−1 for 1.5% GO-LSFT in an air environment at 600 °C. The minimum area-specific resistance (ASR) value was also computed, and the lowest value obtained was 0.02 cm2 for the same sample. Based on 1.5% GO-LSFT-produced cathode material, a peak power density of 362 mW/cm2 was attained.
 Fig. 19  (a) Scheme shows structure and operational conditions for various FCs.319 (b) FC performance at 600 °C. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 430 Copyright (2022), Elsevier. 


6.3.6. Phosphoric acid fuel cells (PAFCs). 
The electrolyte used in PAFCs is an inorganic, chemically and thermally stable liquid H3PO4. Positively charged (H+) ions must be transferred from the electrolyte to the cathode for PAFCs to function. Heat and water are produced at the cathode when (H+) ions interact with electrons and oxygen. Graphite-based BPPs, a Pt/C electrocatalyst over a carbon electrode, and a diffusion layer are components of PAFC stack assemblies.347 The efficiency of PAFCs varies from 40% for pure electricity to 80% for combined power and heat generation. The operating range for PAFCs is 150–220 °C due to the possibility of electrolyte liquefaction at this temperature, where an active mechanism is needed to avoid warmness and electrolyte depletion. PAFCs have several benefits, including excellent stability, resistance to impurities, greater conductivity, and higher power for commercial use.431


6.3.7. Microbial fuel cells (MFCs). 
MFCs are new bio-electrochemical systems (BESs) that convert microbial metabolic energy directly to electricity. MFCs devices are well-known for their ability to generate bioelectricity from biodegradable substrates found in wastewater. Specifically, MFCs are green, sustainable energy sources that generate electricity by using the chemical energy in organic waste, with environmental bioremediation combined with power generation. This is accomplished by microbial metabolism, which releases the e−s trapped in organic substrates and transfers them to solid electrodes via direct or indirect extracellular electron transfer pathways. In principle, the bacterial film in MFC devices acts as an electrocatalyst, converting organic and inorganic materials into electrical current.432 In the event of a closed external circuit by load, the bacteria create the electron and hole transport it to the anode, where electrons travel to the cathode. The produced protons simultaneously travel through the PEM.433
One of the key barriers in the practical use of MFCs is their poor efficiency. Besides the inoculum, the electrode SSA, cell design, proton exchange material, and substrate all have an impact on the performance of MFCs. The anode material has a significant impact on power production because it has the most accessible area for bacterial cells to cultivate, and thus affects the surface electron transfer resistance. Therefore, a high-productivity anode material is critical to further improve the MFC output power performance. The roughness, area, biocompatibility, and potential of anode surfaces all have a substantial impact on the anodic response. The addition of NMs to the anode is one of the most successful strategies to improve the anode performance, which has a beneficial impact on the power production capacities of MFC devices. Because of their outstanding conductivity, vast SSA, and strong mechanical strength, graphene NMs have piqued the curiosity of many researchers. One of these major and promising applications is the direction of MFCs. The low productivity of MFCs compared to other FC technologies and the high cost of their components are the two main barriers preventing their industrialization. Graphene is proposed to assist in overcoming these issues by integrating it with biocatalysts for the construction of graphene-based MFCs, either as an anode to improve electron transfer efficiency or as a cathode to efficiently catalyze the ORR. This integration is only important if there is a conductive environment for bacterial bio-film adhesion to the 2D graphene-based NM modified surfaces (Fig. 20).
 Fig. 20  Scheme showing the structure of MFC (right) focusing on various bacterial e− transfer methods by secreted mediators, pili/NWs and/or bacterial surface cytochromes (left) and G-NM-based anode (right) compared to G-NMs based cathode (left) with respect to highest power density. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 434 Copyright (2022), MDPI. 
To increase the performance of MFCs, PEMs must have exceptional features.435 Because the performance of pure polymeric membranes has been shown to be restricted, scientists have focused on the effective functionalization of polymer or inorganic fillers to optimize the existing efficiency of MFCs. Besides Nafion, several polymers have been identified as potential electrolytes for MFCs; however, a few of them have been shown to be unsuccessful with respect to thermal and electrochemical stability, gas permeability, tensile strength, and ionic conductivity, resulting in their rejection. Alternatively, the use of inorganic fillers and their combination with other polymers can help to eliminate some of the issues associated with the rejected PEMs. For example, the addition of graphene-NMs to PVA-based electrolytes has enhanced tensile strength and proton conductivity. Khilari et al.436 used solution casting to create a graphene-NM-based modified PVA silicotungstic acid (PVA-STA) membrane for MFCs. They created a 100 μm-thick membrane by synthesizing GO NMs using a modified Hummers' technique and a PVA-STA solution. According to electron-impedance-spectroscopy (EIS), the addition of 2D graphene NMs increased the conductivity of the PVA-STA-GO membrane to 0.065 S cm−1; meanwhile, the conductivity of the pure PVA-STA membrane was about 0.046 S cm−1 and that of the Nafion-117 membrane was 0.062 S cm−1. Furthermore, the inclusion of GO NMs resulted in improved mechanical characteristics, i.e., 9.1, 31.3, and 37 MPa for the PVA-STA-GO, Nafion-117, and PVA-STA membranes, respectively. Furthermore, the power density of the GO-NM-based filled membrane was good at 1.19 W m−3, while the power density of Nafion-117 was 0.88 W m−3. Furthermore, Leong et al.437 created a PEM with a graphene-NM-based modification for MFCs. The authors created a GO/SPEEK NCM by using SL GO-NMs as a nano-filler in SPEEK. To determine the functional benefits of the graphene-NMs contained in the polymer matrix, the GO-NM-filled membranes were compared to Nafion-117 and pure SPEEK membranes. The results showed that the GO/SPEEK-based NCMs had the best selectivity, proton conductivity of 1.48 × 10−3 S cm−1, oxygen diffusion coefficient of 1.154 × 10−6 cm2 s−1, and water uptake of 85.4%. Furthermore, the GO/SPEEK-based NCMs exhibited the maximum Coulombic efficiency (16.88%). In addition, the power density of the GO/SPEEK NCM was 902 mW m−2, which was higher than that of pure SPEEK membrane (812 mW m−2). However, in MFCs, the Nafion-117 membrane achieved the maximum power density of 1013 mW m−2.437 Because of its equivalent power density and good efficiency, the GO/SPEEK NCM is a potential and less expensive alternative to the Nafion-117 membrane for MFCs.

7. Applications of graphene nanomaterials for bipolar plate uses in fuel cells
The operational stability and endurance of FC devices are crucial for their practical use. The bipolar plates (BPPs) used in FC stacks typically corrode during oxidation, resulting in less stable devices with poor performances. In FCs, BPPs are employed for current collection, gas and heat distribution, and mechanical support. The BPPs should have high electrical and thermal conductivity, low interfacial contact resistance, low gas permeability, high corrosion resistance, and high mechanical strength, and be lightweight and inexpensive. Graphite is the most commonly used material for BPPs in PEMFCs because of its elevated corrosion resistance and high conductivity (103 S cm−1), but its mechanical strength is low and its fabrication costs are relatively high. Metallic BPPs have high electrical conductivity and mechanical strength, but they are not corrosion resistant in acidic environments. Because of these drawbacks, alternative BPPs with graphene modifications have been developed. As a protective coating for metallic BPPs, anti-corrosive graphene has been employed.
The performance of ordinary Cu and Ni-BPPs degrades with time. Alternatively, graphene-containing BPPs have been shown to be more durable and steady in operation. Consequently, graphene-based materials are a viable option for the BPPs in FCs. Through current–voltage–power (I–V–P) experiments, Lee et al.438 examined the performance of CVD-based graphene coated on Cu as BPPs. As shown in Fig. 21(a)–(d), the results verified that the graphene-coated Cu-BPPs in a single cell outperformed pure Cu-BPPs. The reduced efficiency of a single FC device employing a Cu-BP panel is caused by the surface oxidation of Cu. Furthermore, after 5 h of exposure, Cu-BPP exhibited an increased “Rs” and poor performance. In contrast, the graphene-coated Cu-BPP performed better than the pure Cu-BPP. The graphene coating protected the Cu plate from oxidation and boosted its longevity without sacrificing its performance. As a result, graphene-coated Cu-BPP is a viable option for the fabrication of high-performance, low-cost, long-lasting FC devices.
 Fig. 21  (a) Anode/cathode based on Cu-BPPs, (b) SEM investigations without and (c) with graphene layer on Cu-BPP, (d) polarization curves of the polymer electrode fuel,440 comparative analysis of (e) corrosion current densities and ICRs, (f) power densities of graphene-incorporated and graphene-free BPPs, (g) graphic sketch of PEMFC with Gr/Ni foam-embedded graphite-based BPP, (h) performance analysis of PEMFC with different BPPs,441 electrical conductivities of (i) PPS/GNP and (j) PPS/CB/GNP composites made by ball milling and compression molding,442 and (k) FC with BPPs. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 403 Copyright (2018), the American Chemical Society. 
Sim et al.439 studied the long-term operational stability of 3D graphene-coated Ni-foam (NiF) ((3D-G/NiF) for BPP applications. Thermal annealing for a brief period produced highly crystalline ML 3D graphene coatings from poly(methyl methacrylate). In comparison to previous BPPs, the generated 3D-graphene/NiF retained a low corrosion current density after exposure to the operating environment. Furthermore, at a cell potential of 0.5 V, a hydrogen/air FC device with 3D-graphene/NiF BPPs achieved the highest power density of 967 mW cm−2.
According to the findings in Fig. 21(e)–(h), 3D-graphene can be employed as a protective layer for long-lasting BPPs in high-performance FC devices. Kakati et al.443 described natural graphite (NG), carbon black, carbon fiber (CF), and graphene polymer NC for BPPs in their article. To analyze the influence of NCs, the device performance, electrical conductivity of the BPP, corrosion current, flexural strength, and deflection at mid-span tests were done. The incorporation of CF with graphene in these NC BPPs resulted in a considerable increase in flexural strength from 45.97 to 54.23 and 57.28 MPa. Similarly, the deflection at mid-span changed from 3.03–5.2% and then up to 5.85%. The only difference is that the inclusion of CF reduced the conductivity of the BPPs. The inclusion of graphene improved the in-plane and through-plane conductivities, which varied from 424.96 to 435.31 and 115.71 to 130.17 S cm−1, respectively. The modification with CF increased the corrosion current, whereas the modification with graphene had no such impact. Because of the increased electrical conductivity of the graphene-based modified BPPs, the device performance was boosted from 397 to 437 mW cm−2 and the current density to 752–827 mA cm−2.
Jiang et al.444 investigated the use of graphene nanoplatelets (G-NPs) as the primary filler content in BPPs. As a small filler, poly phenylene sulphide (PPS) with CB was used. The flexure strength of the resulting PPS/G-NP- and PPS/CB/G-NP-based NCs increased monotonically with an increase in the G-NP concentration. Furthermore, enhanced permeability resistance and in-plane conductivities were recorded for both NCs. Fig. 21(i) and (j) depict the electrical conductivities of the NCs. Here, we conclude with graphene nanostructures as potential future uses for BPPs in electrochemical energy conversion devices. However, there are still obstacles in the use of graphene materials in BPPs. It was discovered that graphene had no progress in corrosion resistance at high temperature (>60 °C) for stainless steel plates without Ni coating or for Al-plates, which can be attributed to the galvanic corrosion mechanism or the reaction between ions and defect/grain boundaries in graphene. Because of the poor electrical conductivity of GO- and rGO-based coatings, the interfacial contact resistance may increase. Furthermore, the adhesion of hydrophobic graphene-NMs to metallic plates without the use of a binder is difficult. The binder invariably increases the interfacial contact resistance and expense. Another problem is creating gas flow pathways in graphene-based NMs without compromising their electrical and corrosion-resistant qualities.
For the use of FCs, especially PEMFCs for automotive applications in the late twentieth century, BPPs have continuously developed and innovated. However, further advances in stack power density to 6.0 kW l−1 and 9.0 kW l−1 will present further technological hurdles, still requiring a 20% contribution of power density from BPPs. In this section, we also explain the technical constraints of BPPs and address the basic issues involved in specific technical designs, in conjunction with publications from the US DOE and also Japan NEDO.19,445 Using these studies as examples, and Toyota and Honda products, we offer a complete review of the technological issues faced during the last two decades,30,446,447 as shown in Fig. 22. As a result of their long-term progress and outstanding advancement, both carbon and metal BPPs have been investigated. The mass transfer capacity is a critical parameter in the design of BPPs, which is mostly determined by the flow field structure.
 Fig. 22  Yearly developments in BPPs for FCVs. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 19 Copyright (2021), Springer Nature. 
As illustrated in Fig. 22, there are now two proven design paths for BPPs. The first involves modifying and narrowing their channel-rib structure, while the second involves developing flow fields without ribs but with micro-baffles or porous structures. These pathways offer several advantages over traditional designs, but they also introduce additional challenges. The 3D fine-mesh BPPs of the first-generation MIRAI, for example, displayed effective mass transfer, with baffles stamped in a fish-scale pattern. Alternatively, the tearing process results in fracture surface fractures, exposing the metallic substrate to an acidic electrochemical environment. Additionally, incremental stamping of improved meshes increase the cost of BPPs. The US DOE expects BPPs to achieve permeability to hydrogen and oxygen of less than 2 × 10−6 m2 by 2020, although present flow channels/fields with a depth of less than 0.5 mm may easily achieve a permeability of 10−8 m2. In the second-generation MIRAI, Toyota has replaced the 3D flow field with a 2D wavy channel. Recent BP design revisions suggest that the mass transfer capacity may not be a serious problem for contemporary stacks. However, there is still a gap between the current state (4.4 kW l−1 for second-generation MIRAI) and the power and current density targets (set by Japan NEDO). A simultaneous increase in the gas flow rate may worsen the pressure drop and mal-distribution in the flow channels/fields, resulting in an increase in parasitic power loss, local fuel famine, and hence mass-transfer loss.
Another concern is the liquid removal caused by the high rate of electrochemical water formation. Although improved MEAs can work at lower humidity levels, water condensation and build-up are still difficult to prevent, especially at current densities of 2 A cm−2 and above.448 However, at an operating temperature above 100 °C, evaporation may ameliorate this issue significantly. In summary, improving the mass transfer remains a major problem for the design of BPPs for next-generation PEMFCs. Other problems with the BPP design are related to heat and electron conduction. Given that the amount of heat evacuated via mass flow or dissipated from the cell edges is comparatively small, the majority of the waste heat must be transmitted through the MEA and BPPs before being removed by the external radiator coolant by thermal convection.449 The BPP has a thermal conductivity roughly 30-times that of the GDL or CL for a stack with a power density of around 4 kW l−1, but the interfacial thermal resistance between the BPPs and GDL can be about 10-times that of BPPs.450 The electrical resistance between the BPPs and GDL (about 10−6 m2) is roughly 4-orders of magnitude greater than that in BPPs (approximately 10−10 m2). Despite the fact that modern metal separators can readily achieve thermal and electrical conductivity values of 50 W m−1 K−1 and 1.4 × 106 S m−1, respectively, which are significantly greater than the US DOE standards for 2020, their interfacial resistance is equivalent. The Japan NEDO requested that the output voltage and operating temperature be increased from their existing levels of 0.65V and 90–100 °C to 0.85 V and 120 °C in 2040, respectively. Thus, the primary goal is to lower the thermal and electrical interfacial contact resistance between the BPPs and GDL, which are affected by the cell stack compactness, contact area between the BPPs and MEA, BPP surface roughness, and substrate conductivity. Furthermore, for the construction of a compact cell, the current coolant channels must be combined with hydrogen and/or air distributors, implying a trade-off between increasing the cooling capacity and decreasing thickness.
The durability of BPPs is primarily impacted by their electrochemical corrosion and mechanical deterioration. A further increase in current density (about 3.8 A cm−2 by 2030, according to the Japan NEDO) may result in an increase in the electrochemical corrosion of the components, which is the primary cause of cell failure, particularly for metal BPPs. Corrosion also happens in carbon; however, it is essentially non-existent under the typical working settings. To prevent corrosion, the BPP is usually coated with passive materials capable of creating low-resistance oxide layers or by deposition of corrosion-resistant coatings. The homogeneity of defect-free coatings, stable passive films, or nitride layers451 determines the corrosion resistance efficiency. The corrosion current density of the anode and cathode BPPs should be restricted to 1 A cm−2 in 2020452 according to the US DOE guidelines. Existing coatings on SS 316L (or graphite), such as multi-layer chromium carbide and amorphous carbon, already satisfy these criteria. However, sophisticated coating techniques are still required for improving the corrosion resistance, lowering the contact resistance, and crucially, reducing the coating cost.453,454 Mechanical swelling and shrinking of the MEA, and gas and coolant pressure changes can cause load and pressure fluctuations in metal BPPs, resulting in plastic deformation and fatigue failure.455 The 2020 US DOE flexural and impact strength standards for BPPs are 25 MPa and 40.5 J m−1, respectively. These characteristics have previously been addressed by typical BPP substrate materials such as stainless steel (by Honda456) and flexible graphite (by Ballard457). However, during the production process or long-term operation, localized thickness loss, fractures, and susceptibility to plastic deformation are expected. Furthermore, high compact pressure can cause the deformation of BPPs and their poor contact with the GDL458 in refined ribs, ultrathin plates, meshes, baffles, and metal ligaments.
Although reaching the desired transport capabilities, the manufacturing capacity of BPPs should match the industrial basis for the mass manufacture of FC products. Relying on the substrate material, production capacity, and coating technique,459 the cost and volume of BPPs can account for more than 30% and 70% of the whole PEMFC stack, respectively. The US DOE requirement for the total BPP cost in 2020, including materials, fabrication, and coating is US $3 kW−1. However, the cost of the substrate material alone, for example, SS 316L, is over US $2.7 kW−1, making this goal difficult to attain. Moreover, although the use of ultra-refined plates has recently become a popular strategy for the design of BPPs, it necessitates high-precision and quick manufacturing procedures for metal or graphite substrates, such as progressive stamping and compression moulding. The precision and consistent fabrication of both BPPs and coatings is expected to create significant technological challenges for high-volume production. Consequently, both the Japan NEDO and the United States DOE have highlighted the importance of cost reduction for BPPs for the advancement of FC technology and the FCV industry.19
7.1. Integrated porous bipolar plate-MEA design
Recently, a new type of BPPs w proposed, which uses metal/graphene porous foam as a reactant distributor.460 Due to their appropriate mechanical characteristics, porous BPPs with a reduced volume and weight can attain by uniform mass and heat distribution.461 These porous materials can be manufactured at a much lower cost than refined ribs or baffles, and their geometric parameters, including porosity, pore density and pore shape are controllable.462 This may permit the complete elimination of the GDL, where its substitution with a porous material for mass transport between the CL and external environment that is an integrated porous BPP-MEA design. Additionally to provide a very compact FC, this integration may beneficially remove the interfacial transport of mass, heat and e−s in the BPPs and GDL, thus avoiding interfacial transport resistances. Tanaka et al.463 developed a GDL-free FC device using a corrugated SS mesh flow channel rather than a porous material and a freestanding MPL, indicating the probability of integration. Park et al.462 studied graphene foam to manufacture a unified flow field/GDL lacking an MPL, where the FC thickness was reduced by 82%, while achieving a high power output over a broad current density range. Even though an enhanced performance was attained with a thinner cell in the integrated BPP-MEA design, porous flow field materials with high conductivity are vulnerable to chemical corrosion in an acidic environment. Therefore, effective coating materials and coating methods for 3D porous structures are essential to achieve long-term operational stability. Furthermore, porous materials reveal a lower stiffness than conventional-based BPPs. A BPPs stiffness improvement process, such as compression, is essential to avoid over-deformation of the flow field. In summary, integrated BPP-MEA or GDL-less design can concurrently develop mass transport, alleviate water flooding and decrease the volume of PEMFC stacks, and thus may present a promising approach to achieve an ultrahigh power density (9 kW l−1), owing to the considerable improvement in stack compactness.19
8. Graphene as electrocatalysts in fuel cells
The ORR reaction at the cathode and hydrogen oxidation reaction (HOR) at the anode power hydrogen FCs (HFCs). However, the ORR has been the focus of the graphene electrocatalyst-driven HFC research over the last decade. According to reports, the processes at the anode and cathode in FCs are catalyzed by noble metals (including Pt, Ru, Pd, and Au), non-precious metal (e.g., Ni), and their alloys. Pt is the one of them that is most usually utilized as an electrode reaction electrocatalyst, particularly for FCs with low operating temperature.464 The drawbacks of these noble metals include their high cost, limited availability, poor reaction kinetics, low resistance to CO-poisoning, and crossover effects originating from fuel molecules diffusing from the anode to cathode.465 By adding an electrocatalyst support, the electro-active SSA and catalytic activities can be enhanced, the cycle stability and durability boosted, and the electrode cost reduced. Due to its low cost, carbon black is frequently chosen as a support for numerous FCs.466 Other non-carbon-467 and carbon-based materials, such as active pours-carbon,468 CNTs,469 graphite,470 and carbon aerogels,471 have been suggested as electrocatalyst supports. These electrocatalyst supports must have strong coupling with electrocatalysts, superior chemical and electrochemical stability in the electrolyte, with a big SSA for the even dispersion of the catalyst, and be reasonably priced.467,472 Large-SSA graphene can support and distribute electrocatalyst NPs uniformly to prevent inter-particle accumulation and increase the exposure of the electro-active area. Topographic flaws (for instance, vacancies, holes, edges, and distortion) and residual functional groups (for example, carboxyl, epoxy, and hydroxyl) are frequently found in GO-derived graphene materials, which serve as an ideal substrate for electrocatalyst growth and immobilization with outstanding structural stability and interactions.473 The charge transfer from nanocatalysts to graphene and mass movement over the electrode surface may be enhanced, which lead to elevated conductivity, and the electrochemical stability of graphene coupled with its topographic flaws and residual functional groups can lead to high conversion efficiency.
As previously indicated, when graphene is used as an electrocatalyst support, the electro-catalytic activity for both the methanol oxidation reaction (MOR) and ethanol oxidation reaction (EOR) can be improved. Numerous studies have been conducted, particularly on Pt electro-catalyst hybrids with graphene-NMs. According to the findings, the increased implementation and activation of Pt on graphene NSs led to greater Pt on graphene NS dispersion because the graphene NSs had a bigger SSA. Additionally, they observed the microstructure of graphene, which includes flaws, functional groups, and interaction with Pt clusters.474 The tests performed to evaluate the performance of graphene NMs as electrocatalyst supports are listed below.
M/rGO (M = Pd, Pt, Au–Pd, Pd–Pt, Pd–Pt@Pt and Pt–Ag),475,476 MOx/rGO,477 and metal-free foreign element-doped graphene-based NM electrocatalysts demonstrated consistent activity in the ORR, as evidenced by their high current density output. Carbon materials with high SSA are thought to be effective electrocatalyst supports in FC devices. Doped graphene-NM derivatives with higher conductivity and charge transfer capabilities are predicted to increase the performance of FC devices when used as electrocatalyst supports. Tennyson et al.478 described boron (B)-doped graphene as a potential Pt electrocatalyst support and electrode in a solid acid FC (SAFC). The B-doped graphene NFs, created using pulsed laser ablation, performed well in a 250 °C SAFC (with cesium dihydrogen phosphate electrolyte). The decrease in graphene-NMs e− density caused by B-doping slowed oxygen chemisorption and stabilized the carbon electrodes in the hostile environment of the SAFC.
Kabir et al.479 investigated the performance of N-doped graphene in FCs as an electrode. They experimented with different N-moieties in the 2D graphene NMs and compared their ORR activity. Doped graphene-NMs with high amounts of hydrogenated-N and low levels of pyridinic-N exhibited the best current density, onset potential and half-wave potential. The authors postulated the ORR mechanism for each N-moiety based on their experimental results. Dong et al.480 observed the improved ORR performance of porous graphene foam doped with different elements, i.e., B, N, P, and Fe. H3BO3, (C6H5)2P, CN2H2, and FeCl2 were used as precursors, which were hard-templated with multi-doped graphene-NMs. To validate their practical application, the scientists investigated the electrode stability in various fluids. The negligible drop in half-wave potential revealed the enhanced stability of the electrocatalyst in both acidic and alkaline electrolytes when evaluated at a constant potential. The multiple element-doped graphene foam demonstrated better catalytic activity than its single or dual-doped equivalents due to its high SSA, mesoporous structure, and synergistic effects.
Different forms of FCs contain Pt NPs attached to doped graphene-based NMs as electrocatalysts. One of the major difficulties for DMFCs is the MOR, which has been claimed to be improved by Pt-NPs supported on P–S co-doped graphene.481 Scientists deduced from DFT simulations that the creation of the P–C bonds improved the interaction between Pt and carbon by doping as a result of the change in charge density. In comparison to single-element-doped graphene-based NMs, the co-doped electrocatalyst showed greater electrochemical SSA, superior MOR activity and stability. Reda et al.482 theoretically studied the ORR electrocatalytic activity of N–G NMs. The authors noticed that the *O and *O2 adsorbates were more stabilized on the basal plane. They concluded through DFT calculations that the increased free N and solvation effects were responsible for the better electrocatalyst function than metal-based electrocatalysts.
Kabir et al.483 investigated the ORR catalytic activity of 3D N-doped graphene-supported Pd NCs for AEMFCs. Compared to Pd/carbon-black and other graphene NCs, the 3D-doped electrode had a greater onset potential for the ORR and a higher power density. The increased mass transfer kinetics and electrical characteristics of the support were responsible for the improved performance. Soft template assistance was used to generate co-doped N–S graphene-NMs using C3H6N6 and benzyl disulfide. The substance demonstrated strong CH4O tolerance and long-term FC electrocatalytic durability. Also, its high SSA and porous nano-architecture resulted in an improved performance. When combined with N-doped graphene, TM oxy-hydroxides also showed improved ORR activity. Li et al.484 presented as an example, a porous N-graphene encapsulated b-FeOOH hybrid. The surface FeO6 locations were converted into active sites, lowering the free energy gap of the ORR and improving the catalytic efficacy.
In acidic, neutral, and basic environments, the hybrid performed better than the commercial Pt/carbon electrocatalyst when used as a cathode in both MFCs and straight DMFCs. The TMs and oxides in doped graphene are frequently linked to the heteroatoms in the carbon network and have a promising future as FC electrode NMs. For instance, Kim et al.485 reported the use of N-doped graphene nano-platelets containing Fe for PEMFCs operating in acidic environments. Varga et al.486 examined the electrocatalytic activity of Co4N/N-graphene hybrids for AFCs in a recent research. Comparing the NCs to a commercial Pt/C electrocatalyst, they showed their greater tolerance to CH4O. Chuong et al.487 suggested that a ternary hybrid based on Fe2O3, MoS2, and N-graphene can solve the issue of CH4O crossover in cathodic electrocatalysts. An electrocatalyst with a high electron-transfer number (3.91–3.96) showed excellent operating stability. Pt/Au/Pt core/shell nanocrystals with 2D N-doped graphene NMs as a support were created by Xu et al.488 for the electrocatalytic oxidation of formic acid. This material, according to the scientists, may be regarded as one of the most promising electrocatalysts due to its increased mass and specific activity. Due to its high SSA and high electrolyte-accessible internal volume, the doped graphene displayed strong ORR activity and high power density. As an example, Wang et al.489 employed a graphene NM-based mesh/Co3O4 electrocatalyst that was B/N co-doped in a KOH solution (Fig. 23(a)–(f)).
 Fig. 23  (a) CVs of GM–Co–B–N in oxygen and N-saturated electrolyte. (b) Different polarization curves at 1600 rpm in oxygen saturated electrolyte. (c) LSVs of GM–Co–B–N from 400–2500 rpm, inset shows the KL plot of GM–Co–B–N. (d) e− transfer number of the catalysts, (e) durability of the electrodes, (f) current density loss-time CA responses at 0.3 V in oxygen saturated electrolyte at 1200 rpm,490 and CVs of Pd-Cu NCs/rGO (g), rGO (h) and Pd black (i) modified electrodes without (curve a) and with (curve b) 3.0 M CH4O in 0.1 M KOH at 50 mV s−1. Reprinted (adapted) with permission from ref. 491 Copyright (2014), Elsevier. 
Some study teams additionally looked at the crossover impact on the cathodic electrode ORR performance when alcohol (such as CH4O) is present. The outcome demonstrated that the graphene-based ORR electrocatalysts had strong tolerance to CH4O when added to the cathode area compared to the benchmark Pt/C cathode. This was also shown by the representative outcome exhibited by Prof. Wang's group in Zn-air battery research using a B/N co-doped graphene NM-based nano-mesh/Co3O4 electrocatalyst in KOH. Xie's group also used PtNCs@NGA to catalyze the ORR in H2SO4,492 and Lv's group used Pd-Cu NCs/rGO to catalyze the ORR in KOH493 (Fig. 23(g)–(i)). Similar to alcohol FC research, it is uncommon to see graphene-NM-based electrocatalyst-driven HFC or metal-air batteries at the device scale. Wang's group selected B/N co-doped graphene nano-mesh/Co3O4 as the cathodic material and MOx/rGO as the electrode material for a ZAB as a good illustration of this. After lengthy maintenance for 10 h, the battery displayed equivalent activity to the benchmark Pt/C cathode-driven ZABs, as evidenced by the battery performance during a single cycle of prolonged operation.489 In FCs, the cathode typically includes the reduction of O from the air, while the anode is associated with the oxidation of fuels (for example hydrogen, CH4O and CH2O2). The FC graphene hybrid electro-catalysts are shown in Table 12.

In a study on Pt/graphene electrocatalysts, Lei et al.521 produced electrocatalysts with the help of poly-diallyl dimethyl-ammonium (PDDA). The PEMFC performance and electrochemical stability were found to be very good. The uniform distribution of Pt-NPs on the surface of graphene was caused by the existence of PDDA, which improved the electrocatalytic activity. Ji et al.522 conducted a different analysis in which an effective and clean procedure was utilized, where ascorbic acid induced the reduction of the GO-NSs and Pt-NPs in a single step to create Pt-NPs attached to graphene-NSs. In comparison to regular Vulcan XC-72, these graphene-NM-supported Pt nano-clusters demonstrated higher electrocatalytic activity and stability for the electro-oxidation of CH4O. The Pd-NP electrocatalyst was removed using the pulse microwave-assisted polyol (MP) approach by Hsieh et al.523 from a graphene NS electrode for PEMFC with a variable pH. In contrast to employing GO NS, this MP approach used more graphene-based NS in a shorter time, where PEFCs were produced using a sub-nano-Pt anode electrocatalyst on GNS, according to a research by Yoo et al.524 In the hydrogen oxidation process (HOR), Pt electrocatalysts on graphene NSs were more CO-tolerant than Pt electrocatalysts on carbon black (Pt/CB). In the work by Liu et al.,525 graphene was written on the carbon fabric of the MFC anodes. Due to the great biocompatibility of graphene, the power density and energy conversion were enhanced. This advances bacterial development on the electrode surface and speeds up the transmission of electrons. Employing graphene as electro-catalysts still has problems to solve, such as electrocatalyst poisoning or corrosion, self-accumulation and oxidation of the electrocatalyst, and support in operational circumstances. However, due to the abundance of active sites in graphene, which aids in the uniform dispersion of electrocatalysts, utilizing doped-graphene or functionalized graphene as electro-catalyst supports for metal or oxide electrocatalysts can increase their electrocatalytic activity performance.179
In DMFCs, the MOR attracted the most research attention in alcoholic fuel electro-oxidation. Metal-loaded reduced graphene (M/rGO) and metal-loaded graphene aerogel (M/GA) have been the widely used electrocatalytic materials for the MOR in the last ten years. For 2D M/rGO or 3D graphene anodic electrocatalysts, Pt is the most often employed metal candidate. The Pt-loaded reduced graphene electrocatalysts (Pt/rGO or Pt/GA based) show stronger electrochemical activity in MeOH half-cell-scaled electro-oxidation than the commercial Pt/C, Pt/carbon-black or even pure Pt, according to results from various groups. This is demonstrated by the highest anodic sweep current density (Jf) of about 2540 A g−1 (unit mass)500 or 30 mA cm−2 (unit area).526 However, EtOH has emerged as a substitute fuel in the electro-oxidation investigation due to the toxicity of CH4O. Similar to the MOR, Pt-based graphene electrocatalysts have been widely employed in recent years and demonstrated significant activity in the EOR.527,528 The metal candidate was later replaced by a non-Pt monometallic or bimetallic electrocatalyst for the elimination of CO poisoning from the MOR and EOR in Pt/graphene anodic electrocatalysts due to the CO molecules blocking the active surface sites of Pt, which reduces the lifetime of the Pt/graphene electrocatalysts. Non-Pt mono-metallic electrocatalysts, such as, Pd, (2D M/rGO), Ni (2D M/rGO, 3D M/GF and M/GA), Cu, Ag, Au, and bimetallic catalysts (2D M/rGO and 3D M/GA or M/GF) have become alternatives.
These graphene electrocatalysts loaded with metals demonstrated significantly higher electrochemical activity in half-cell-scaled MOR (2D M/rGO and 3D M/GF), EOR (2D M/rGO and 3D M/GF and M/GA), butan-1-ol electro-oxidation (butan-1-ol OR),529 glucose electro-oxidation (GOR) (2D M/rGO and 3D M/GA) and formic acid electro-oxidation. In the MOR and EOR, these graphene-based electrocatalysts also outperformed monometallic Pt/graphene electrocatalysts. This was reflected in the high anodic peak current density observed from the best output current density of 4972 A g−1500 or 252 mA cm−2516 in the MOR, 2105.4 A g−1530 or 2219 mA cm−2531 in the EOR, and the best If/Ib ratio of 11.65 in the MOR530 and 5.45 in the EOR.532
Besides the MOR and EOR, butan-1-ol OR,529 FOR,533,534 and GOR535 catalyzed by graphene electrocatalysts have received attention in recent years. However, systematic research in this area is limited in comparison to the MOR and EOR, and non-Pt and bimetallic M/graphene electrocatalysts.536 M/rGO or M/GA-based electrocatalysts exhibit strong activity in the butan-1-ol OR (highest Jf:15.59 mA cm−2 or 105.98 mA mg−1125), FOR (max Jf: 61.6 mA cm−2533 or 3390 A g−1537), and GOR (max Jf:16.2 mA cm−2 (500–1248 cycles). To date, the majority of graphene electro-catalyst research has been done on a secondary substrate platform, for instance, glassy carbon electrode (GCE). These efforts involved the use of binding or stabilizing agents such as Nafion solution with ultrasonic handling for the manufacture of electrocatalyst inks. This approach has the unintended consequence of damaging the structure of graphene, which is especially noticeable for 3D graphene nanostructures. The use of a conductive binder also results in the binder reducing the catalytic active surface. A recent study showed that the use of a binder-free graphene electrode to increase the activity is an option. Binder-free graphene-NM-based electrodes provide various advantages over regular electrodes, as follows.
(1) Compared to the use of non-conductive and active binders, the specific capacity and conductivity of the electrode can be enhanced.
(2) The e− transport in the electrode can be improved by interconnecting or stacking graphene through self-assembly, forming a highly conductive and resilient network.
(3) Because of the great SSA of the electrode, the effective contact at the electrolyte and electrode interface reduces the diffusion resistance.538
Following the binder-free production of pure GA/NF as a direct SC electrode, the same chemical route was used to create a metal-loaded GA/NF direct electrode. High activity was reported for the MOR (max Jf = 798.8 A g−1) and EOR (max Jf = 862 A g−1) for the binder-free Pd/GA/NF electrode539 and EOR (max Jf = 3408.7 A g−1)540 and GOR (Ja2 = 379.4 A g−1541 for the binder-free Pd1Ptx/GA/NF. This discovery was exceptional in comparison to others made using typical electrode preparation procedures, with elevated stability of 150 cycles over 1248 cycles in the prolonged GOR and 100–500 cycles over 1456 cycles in the prolonged EOR. In comparison to the previously indicated basic half-cell-sized electrode study, a systematic investigation of graphene electrocatalysts in the FC unit is limited.542 The types of FC units were also restricted to DEFC543 and direct glucose FC (DGFC) units, with the findings demonstrating that the output power density and current density were greater than the existing non-graphene electrocatalyst-driven DEFC and DGFC units. Notably, the binder-free Pd1Ptx/GA/NF electrode plate-driven DEFC and DGFC units performed better than the Nafion-modified Pd1Ptx/GA/NF plate in the same DEFC (29.4% binder-free) and DGFC (66.5% binder-free) under similar fuel combination and working environments.
9. Conclusion and future prospects
Currently, graphene is an attractive material because of its superb electron transport capabilities and other characteristics, which its production developing swiftly. Considerable efforts have been devoted to improving the chemical and physical characteristics of graphene and its derivatives (i.e., GO, rGO, doped graphene, and functionalized graphene) for applications in FC technology. Herein, we summarized the latest performance and advancement in the use of graphene-based NMs in membranes, BPPs, and as electrocatalysts in many types of FC technology applications. The graphene dispersion over electrodes has exposed the enhanced introduction of electro-chemically active specific surface area for higher electrocatalytic activity toward fuel oxidation and oxidant reduction processes. The problems of device stack durability and performance deterioration caused by corrosion of the BPP are emphasized by employing materials based on graphene.
GO-based NMs offer a framework for investigating the relationships between the property and structure of polymer NCs. To improve the processibility, chemical, electrochemical, and mechanical characteristics of membranes, graphene is a superb material to mix with other polymers. In this review, we also methodically emphasized functionalized graphene as a viable membrane material for FCs, also enumerating the prospective benefits and most current developments in the production of graphene-based membranes and their impacts on the functionality of FCs. Proton conductivity, ionic conductivity, water absorption, decrease in CH4O permeability, IEC, ionic transport, swelling ratio, and proton transport are essential features of PEMs in FCs, which are positively altered by GO-NMs. It has been suggested that GO-NMs or their functionalized version be employed for membrane alterations in FCs because, in contrast to graphene, their attached oxygen groups (GO) are easily dispersible in common solvents. GO is primarily produced via the Hummer's technique, and subsequently GO-NMs may be further functionalized into other forms. According to studies, functionalized graphene-NMs are the most desirable fillers and materials with the most potential for use in FCs. GO-based NMs can be sulfonated or carboxylated chemically to enhance their properties even further. The mystery surrounding some parameters, such as the C/O ratio, water content, effective percentage as a filler, and the degree of sulfonation of GO, remains unsolved despite all the work reported on GO-based membranes. This calls for more focused research to examine the behavioral changes in GO as a filler with various polymer matrices. In addition, functionalizing ILs with GO filler has been proven to be more efficient for membrane modification; consequently, the use of GO-based fillers may one day prove to be an intriguing and successful technique to change the many properties of NCMs. However, this requires the thorough investigation of various functional groups and how they affect membrane modification. Research on graphene-modified polymer NCMs is accumulating swiftly and offering technical data and design guidance to improve their performance in a variety of energy applications. Studies on graphene-based polymeric membranes have entered a crucial stage of the product lifecycle, where they are being used in a variety of energy applications, with FC being one of the most alluring. The successful use of graphene in membrane alterations will be necessary to improve the existing situation and open up new prospects.
10. Rising issues in the industrial production of graphene nanomaterials and future development of fuel cells
Concerning problem of the uneven quality and characteristics of commercially accessible graphene NMs, there are two important issues. Firstly, as already noted, converting academic advancements into actual industrial development is now somehow difficult due to the need for precise control of the manufacturing methods, procedures, and product quality. Secondly, the graphene industry demands process repeatability, reliability, and clear gradation with respect to intrinsic qualities, performance related parameters, and modern techniques. This requires unified technological standardization, with a stable production process for graded products.
The industrial fabrication of graphene, with a focus on quality, affordability, repeatability, processability, and safety, provides the foundation for the commercialization of graphene NMs. The transition from lab size to pilot scale, and then to industrialization presents additional challenges that cannot be solved with simple mathematics. The structural characteristics of graphene NFs are extremely sensitive to the processing equipment and preparation conditions, which make them difficult to manage on an industrial scale. It is essential to improve the graphene product quality control. Recent studies on commercially available graphene-based NMs have revealed the poor genuine graphene content (less than 50%), low sp2-bond content (less than 60%), and significant levels of contamination, all of which are harmful for future nanotechnological applications. The mass manufacturing of graphene NFs also has to pay greater attention to uniformity and repeatability. For instance, there is a broad range of quality and inherent qualities in raw graphite materials used in exfoliation, which are sourced from diverse sources and often have varying morphologies and contaminants. Small graphene NFs are now only used commercially as additives for NCMs, where uniformity and performance repeatability are not necessary. Pollution issues arise from the use of surfactants, solvent, and graphene NM-based products, and some active oxidizing species are recognized as prone to detonation.544 Therefore, it will be crucial at the industrial scale to produce, store, transport, and apply graphene NFs in a safe and environmentally friendly manner.
Maximizing the total SSA of metal foil in the CVD chamber for graphene production is a clear strategy for increasing the production capacity in B2B processes for the industrial production of CVD graphene films. This is typically accomplished by broadening the CVD equipment or by effectively stacking metal foils. Both the macroscopic and microscopic environments will be altered significantly when scaled up. There would be a noticeable difference in the heat and mass transmission in the directions parallel and vertical to gas flow, especially at the microscopic level. The non-uniform thickness and domain size will drastically worsen the properties of the produced graphene. These problems can be possibly solved by using vertical gas flow, local feeding, or static sealing methods, in which formation chamber is supplied with enough reaction gas, and then sealed once it reaches the growth temperature. The mono-crystallization of the Cu-substrate, which may restrict the unidirectional orientation of the graphene-NM grains towards the creation of larger-size single crystals of graphene-NMs, is another efficient remedy.545 High-quality graphene can take several hours or even a whole day to produce (in big size), and a high-temperature B2B technique often results in a slow growth rate and low yield. Thus, there is a technical trade-off between quality and production speed.
Because the growth substrate is constantly moving during continuous dynamic production, the in-homogeneity of the heat and mass transport in the reaction chamber becomes a more significant component impacting the development. As a result of the restricted residence period of the substrate in the chamber during annealing and graphene-NM development in R2R, the film quality suffers given that the substrates typically move more quickly than graphene throughout its growth. Through the movement of heat and the carbon precursor, the formation of graphene in various places on the moving substrate can affect each another. For instance, downstream,546 graphene is added to the layer that was already created, while upstream, graphene is generated on a new substrate with inadequate annealing time. Some recent academic discoveries, such as mono-crystallization of metal substrates545 and exerting local control over the carbon supply to inhibit excess nucleation, can be used in the R2R technique to create homogeneous single-crystal graphene. Although Cu-foil is still the extensively used substrate for producing graphene thin films in large quantities, it is widely acknowledged that the Cu/Ni-alloy has special features that make it possible to manage the number of layers and domain size and accomplish rapid development.547 The progress of manufacturing methods for this alloy substrate would be further encouraged by the potential to produce high-quality graphene at a reasonable cost. The quality and performance of the produced graphene cost and its processability should be considered when determining the optimal substrate.5–7,10–13,15–17,43,45,52,55,56,60,63,67,69,71,73,548–609
Similar to the Si industry, graphene does not possess infinite potential. The secret to success depends in making advances in the techniques for the synthesis of this new material and identifying its most effective uses. As previously indicated, recent breakthroughs in research have discovered a number of possibilities for potentially fatal uses. Regarding applications in FCs, future GDL and MPL work should focus on optimizing cross-scale and cross-component transport, while remaining compatible with advancements in other components in terms of structural control and wettability. For the CL, innovative catalyst activities are sufficient at the RDE level but require significant progress at the MEA and stack levels. The use of molecular arrangements to modify carbon supports and catalyst/polymer interfaces has the potential to improve the ionomer distribution and catalyst usage. Because it offers great power density at ultralow catalyst loadings, the order-structured MEA is a promising choice. PFSA-based polymers with improved durability and flexibility are predicted to continue to dominate the PEM industry over the next 5–10 years. The long-term purpose of BPP design is to overcome the problems of corrosion, high manufacturing cost, and interfacial contact resistance. For future ultrahigh-power-density operations, the mass transport capacity must be increased. Because of the benefits of interface removal and volume reduction, the combined BP-MEA architecture is projected to give a viable route towards ultrahigh power density.
Although we should appreciate the ongoing attempts to reduce production costs, the cost of commercially available graphene NM-based items should be dictated by the performance of the product. Instead of only emphasizing the supply of inexpensive deals, the graphene research and industry community should concentrate on enhancing the quality and performance of graphene devices. Even if the price was 5-times higher, there would be a sizable demand for a product with a 10-fold increase in performance. Although graphene has been universally acknowledged as a promising 2DM with many intriguing features, it appears impossible to forecast the exact future of graphene business. Graphene industrialization and commercialization are long processes with no shortcuts to success; nonetheless, just a few years have gone from the initial transfer of academic research into practical applications. The graphene sector can have a better future thanks to ongoing advancements in industrial-scale material production and new, game-changing uses. Improving the power densities, lowering prices, and boosting the durability of FC devices will directly encourage large-scale commercialization. These three criteria are linked and restricted by each another, which should be evaluated holistically in the creation of various FC products. Overall, based on the existing framework of materials, building refined and controlled structural designs that can be quickly made is a crucial goal, and the creation of new 2D NMs is predicted to have far-reaching consequences in the long term.
